			<h1 id="firstHeading" class="firstHeading" lang="en">Gender equality</h1>
									<div id="bodyContent" class="mw-body-content">
									<div id="siteSub">From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia</div>
								<div id="contentSub"><div id="mw-fr-revisiontag" class="flaggedrevs_short flaggedrevs_stable_synced plainlinks noprint nomobile"><div class="flaggedrevs_short_basic"><img class="flaggedrevs-icon" src="/w/extensions/FlaggedRevs/frontend/modules/img/doc-magnify.png" alt="Changes must be reviewed before being displayed on this page." title="Changes must be reviewed before being displayed on this page." /><img id="mw-fr-revisiontoggle" class="fr-toggle-arrow" src="/w/extensions/FlaggedRevs/frontend/modules/img/arrow-down.png" style="display:none;" alt="show/hide details" /></div>
<div id="mw-fr-revisiondetails-wrapper" style="position:relative;"><div id="mw-fr-revisiondetails" class="flaggedrevs_short_details" style="display:none">This is the <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Pending_changes" title="Wikipedia:Pending changes">latest accepted revision</a>, <a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Special:Log&amp;type=review&amp;page=Gender_equality">reviewed</a> on <i>9 March 2017</i>.</div>
</div>
</div>
</div>

				</div>
				<div id="mw-content-text" lang="en" dir="ltr" class="mw-content-ltr"><div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Igualtat_de_sexes.svg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2a/Igualtat_de_sexes.svg/170px-Igualtat_de_sexes.svg.png" width="170" height="238" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2a/Igualtat_de_sexes.svg/255px-Igualtat_de_sexes.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2a/Igualtat_de_sexes.svg/340px-Igualtat_de_sexes.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="206" data-file-height="289" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Igualtat_de_sexes.svg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
A generic symbol for gender equality</div>
</div>
</div>
<p><b>Gender equality</b>, also known as <b>sex equality</b>, <b>gender egalitarianism</b>, <b>sexual equality</b>, or <b>equality of the genders</b>, is the belief that everyone should receive <a href="/wiki/Social_equality" title="Social equality">equal treatment</a> and not be <a href="/wiki/Gender_discrimination" class="mw-redirect" title="Gender discrimination">discriminated against based on their gender</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-1">[1]</a></sup> Gender equality is one of the objectives of the United Nations <a href="/wiki/Universal_Declaration_of_Human_Rights" title="Universal Declaration of Human Rights">Universal Declaration of Human Rights</a>. They seek to create equality in law and in social situations, such as in <a href="/wiki/Democracy" title="Democracy">democratic</a> activities and securing <a href="/wiki/Equal_pay_for_equal_work" title="Equal pay for equal work">equal pay for equal work</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-2">[2]</a></sup> The objective of gender equality is for people of different genders to acquire, if they so choose, equal treatment throughout a society, not just in politics, the workplace, or any other policy-designated sphere. To avoid complication, <a href="/wiki/Third_gender" title="Third gender">genders besides women and men</a> will <i>not</i> be discussed in this article.</p>
<p></p>

<p></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Gender_equality&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: History">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p><a href="/wiki/Christine_de_Pizan" title="Christine de Pizan">Christine de Pizan</a>, an early advocate for gender equality, states in her 1405 book <i><a href="/wiki/The_Book_of_the_City_of_Ladies" title="The Book of the City of Ladies">The Book of the City of Ladies</a></i> that the oppression of women is founded on irrational prejudice, pointing out numerous advances in society probably created by women.<sup id="cite_ref-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-3">[3]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Shakers">Shakers</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Gender_equality&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Shakers">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Shakers" title="Shakers">Shakers</a></div>
<div class="thumb tmulti tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:204px;max-width:204px">
<div class="tsingle" style="margin:1px;width:202px;max-width:202px">
<div class="thumbimage"><a href="/wiki/File:Life_of_the_Diligent_Shaker.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9e/Life_of_the_Diligent_Shaker.jpg/200px-Life_of_the_Diligent_Shaker.jpg" width="200" height="142" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9e/Life_of_the_Diligent_Shaker.jpg/300px-Life_of_the_Diligent_Shaker.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/9/9e/Life_of_the_Diligent_Shaker.jpg 2x" data-file-width="400" data-file-height="284" /></a></div>
<div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left"><i>Life of the Diligent Shaker,</i> Shaker Historical Society</div>
</div>
<div class="tsingle" style="margin:1px;width:202px;max-width:202px">
<div class="thumbimage"><a href="/wiki/File:The_Ritual_Dance_of_the_Shakers.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/73/The_Ritual_Dance_of_the_Shakers.jpg/200px-The_Ritual_Dance_of_the_Shakers.jpg" width="200" height="142" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/73/The_Ritual_Dance_of_the_Shakers.jpg/300px-The_Ritual_Dance_of_the_Shakers.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/7/73/The_Ritual_Dance_of_the_Shakers.jpg 2x" data-file-width="400" data-file-height="284" /></a></div>
<div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left"><i>The Ritual Dance of the Shakers,</i> Shaker Historical Society</div>
</div>
<div class="tsingle" style="margin:1px;width:202px;max-width:202px">
<div class="thumbimage"><a href="/wiki/File:The_Shakers_harvesting_their_famous_herbs.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/87/The_Shakers_harvesting_their_famous_herbs.jpg/200px-The_Shakers_harvesting_their_famous_herbs.jpg" width="200" height="142" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/87/The_Shakers_harvesting_their_famous_herbs.jpg/300px-The_Shakers_harvesting_their_famous_herbs.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/8/87/The_Shakers_harvesting_their_famous_herbs.jpg 2x" data-file-width="400" data-file-height="284" /></a></div>
<div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left">The Shakers harvesting their famous herbs</div>
</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Shakers" title="Shakers">Shakers</a>, an evangelical group, which practiced segregation of the sexes and strict <a href="/wiki/Celibacy" title="Celibacy">celibacy</a>, were early practitioners of gender equality. They branched off from a <a href="/wiki/Quaker" class="mw-redirect" title="Quaker">Quaker</a> community in the north-west of England before emigrating to America in 1774. In America, the head of the Shakers' central ministry in 1788, Joseph Meacham, had a revelation that the sexes should be equal. He then brought <a href="/wiki/Lucy_Wright" title="Lucy Wright">Lucy Wright</a> into the ministry as his female counterpart, and together they restructured society to balance the rights of the sexes. Meacham and Wright established leadership teams where each elder, who dealt with the mens' spiritual welfare, was partnered with an eldress, who did the same for women. Each deacon was partnered with a deaconess. Men had oversight of men; women had oversight of women. Women lived with women; men lived with men. In Shaker society, a woman did not have to be controlled or owned by any man. After Meacham's death in 1796, Wright became the head of the Shaker ministry until her death in 1821.</p>
<p>Shakers maintained the same pattern of gender-balanced leadership for more than 200 years. They also promoted equality by working together with other women's rights advocates. In 1859, Shaker Elder Frederick Evans stated their beliefs forcefully, writing that Shakers were "the first to disenthrall woman from the condition of vassalage to which all other religious systems (more or less) consign her, and to secure to her those just and equal rights with man that, by her similarity to him in organization and faculties, both God and nature would seem to demand".<sup id="cite_ref-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-4">[4]</a></sup> Evans and his counterpart, Eldress Antoinette Doolittle, joined women's rights advocates on speakers' platforms throughout the northeastern U.S. in the 1870s. A visitor to the Shakers wrote in 1875:</p>
<dl>
<dd>Each sex works in its own appropriate sphere of action, there being a proper subordination, deference and respect of the female to the male in his order, <i>and of the male to the female in her order</i> [emphasis added], so that in any of these communities the zealous advocates of "women’s rights" may here find a practical realization of their ideal.<sup id="cite_ref-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-5">[5]</a></sup></dd>
</dl>
<p>The Shakers were more than a radical religious sect on the fringes of American society; they put equality of the sexes into practice. They demonstrated that equality was achievable and how to achieve it.<sup id="cite_ref-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-6">[6]</a></sup></p>
<p>In wider society, the movement towards gender equality began with the <a href="/wiki/Suffragette" title="Suffragette">suffrage movement</a> in Western cultures in the late-19th century, which sought to allow women to vote and hold elected office. This period also witnessed significant changes to <a href="/wiki/Women%27s_property_rights" title="Women's property rights">women's property rights</a>, particularly in relation to their marital status. (See for example, <a href="/wiki/Married_Women%27s_Property_Act_1882" title="Married Women's Property Act 1882">Married Women's Property Act 1882</a>.)</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Post-war_era">Post-war era</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Gender_equality&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Post-war era">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Anti-discrimination_laws" class="mw-redirect" title="Anti-discrimination laws">Anti-discrimination laws</a></div>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Timeline_of_women%27s_legal_rights_(other_than_voting)" title="Timeline of women's legal rights (other than voting)">Timeline of women's legal rights (other than voting)</a></div>
<p>After <a href="/wiki/World_War_II" title="World War II">World War II</a>, a more general movement for gender equality developed based on <a href="/wiki/Women%27s_liberation" class="mw-redirect" title="Women's liberation">women's liberation</a> and <a href="/wiki/Feminism" title="Feminism">feminism</a>. The central issue was that <a href="/wiki/Women%27s_rights" title="Women's rights">the rights of women</a> should be the same as of <a href="/wiki/Men%27s_rights" class="mw-redirect" title="Men's rights">men</a>. Feminists believe that women should be equal in every aspect to men and deserve the same rights that are given to men.</p>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/United_Nations" title="United Nations">United Nations</a> and other international agencies have adopted several conventions, toward the promotion of gender equality. Prominent international instruments include:</p>
<ul>
<li>In 1960 the <a href="/wiki/Convention_against_Discrimination_in_Education" title="Convention against Discrimination in Education">Convention against Discrimination in Education</a> was adopted, coming into force in 1962 and 1968.</li>
<li>The <a href="/wiki/Convention_on_the_Elimination_of_All_Forms_of_Discrimination_against_Women" class="mw-redirect" title="Convention on the Elimination of All Forms of Discrimination against Women">Convention on the Elimination of All Forms of Discrimination against Women</a> (CEDAW) is an international treaty adopted in 1979 by the <a href="/wiki/United_Nations_General_Assembly" title="United Nations General Assembly">United Nations General Assembly</a>. Described as an international <a href="/wiki/Bill_of_rights" title="Bill of rights">bill of rights</a> for women, it came into force on 3 September 1981.</li>
<li>The <a href="/wiki/Vienna_Declaration_and_Programme_of_Action" title="Vienna Declaration and Programme of Action">Vienna Declaration and Programme of Action</a>, a <a href="/wiki/Human_rights" title="Human rights">human rights</a> declaration adopted by consensus at the <a href="/wiki/World_Conference_on_Human_Rights" title="World Conference on Human Rights">World Conference on Human Rights</a> on 25 June 1993 in <a href="/wiki/Vienna" title="Vienna">Vienna</a>, <a href="/wiki/Austria" title="Austria">Austria</a>. Women's rights are addressed at para 18.<sup id="cite_ref-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-7">[7]</a></sup></li>
<li>The <a href="/wiki/Declaration_on_the_Elimination_of_Violence_Against_Women" title="Declaration on the Elimination of Violence Against Women">Declaration on the Elimination of Violence Against Women</a> was adopted by the <a href="/wiki/United_Nations_General_Assembly" title="United Nations General Assembly">United Nations General Assembly</a> in 1993.</li>
<li>In 1994, the twenty-year <i>Cairo Programme of Action</i> was adopted at the <a href="/wiki/International_Conference_on_Population_and_Development" title="International Conference on Population and Development">International Conference on Population and Development</a> (ICPD) in <a href="/wiki/Cairo" title="Cairo">Cairo</a>. This <a href="/wiki/Non-binding_resolution" title="Non-binding resolution">non binding</a> programme-of-action asserted that governments have a responsibility to meet individuals' reproductive needs, rather than demographic targets. As such, it called for <a href="/wiki/Family_planning" title="Family planning">family planning</a>, <a href="/wiki/Reproductive_rights" title="Reproductive rights">reproductive rights</a> services, and strategies to promote gender equality and stop violence against women.</li>
<li>Also in 1994, in the Americas, <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://www.oas.org/juridico/english/treaties/a-61.html">The Inter-American Convention on the Prevention, Punishment, and Eradication of Violence against Women</a>, known as the <i>Convention of Belém do Pará</i>, called for the end of violence and discrimination against women.<sup id="cite_ref-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-8">[8]</a></sup></li>
<li>At the end of the <a href="/wiki/Fourth_World_Conference_on_Women" title="Fourth World Conference on Women">Fourth World Conference on Women</a>, the UN adopted the <a href="/wiki/Beijing_Declaration" title="Beijing Declaration">Beijing Declaration</a> on 15 September 1995 - a <a href="/wiki/United_Nations_General_Assembly_resolution" title="United Nations General Assembly resolution">resolution</a> adopted to promulgate a set of principles concerning gender equality.</li>
<li>The <a href="/wiki/United_Nations_Security_Council_Resolution_1325" title="United Nations Security Council Resolution 1325">United Nations Security Council Resolution 1325</a> (UNSRC 1325), which was adopted on 31 October 2000, deals with the rights and protection of women and girls during and after <a href="/wiki/Armed_conflict" class="mw-redirect" title="Armed conflict">armed conflicts</a>.</li>
<li>The <a href="/wiki/Maputo_Protocol" title="Maputo Protocol">Maputo Protocol</a> guarantees comprehensive rights to women, including the right to take part in the political process, to social and political equality with men, to control their <a href="/wiki/Reproductive_health" title="Reproductive health">reproductive health</a>, and an end to <a href="/wiki/Female_genital_cutting" class="mw-redirect" title="Female genital cutting">female genital mutilation</a>. It was adopted by the <a href="/wiki/African_Union" title="African Union">African Union</a> in the form of a protocol to the <a href="/wiki/African_Charter_on_Human_and_Peoples%27_Rights" title="African Charter on Human and Peoples' Rights">African Charter on Human and Peoples' Rights</a> and came into force in 2005.</li>
<li>The EU <a href="/wiki/Directive_(European_Union)" title="Directive (European Union)">directive</a> <i>Directive 2002/73/EC - equal treatment of 23 September 2002 amending Council Directive 76/207/EEC on the implementation of the principle of equal treatment for men and women as regards access to employment, vocational training and promotion, and working conditions</i> states that: "Harassment and sexual harassment within the meaning of this Directive shall be deemed to be discrimination on the grounds of sex and therefore prohibited."<sup id="cite_ref-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-9">[9]</a></sup></li>
<li>The <a href="/wiki/Council_of_Europe" title="Council of Europe">Council of Europe</a>'s <a href="/wiki/Convention_on_preventing_and_combating_violence_against_women_and_domestic_violence" title="Convention on preventing and combating violence against women and domestic violence">Convention on preventing and combating violence against women and domestic violence</a>, the first legally binding instrument in Europe in the field of violence against women,<sup id="cite_ref-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-10">[10]</a></sup> came into force in 2014.</li>
<li>The <a href="/wiki/Council_of_Europe" title="Council of Europe">Council of Europe</a>'s <i>Gender Equality Strategy 2014-2017</i>, which has five strategic objectives:<sup id="cite_ref-11" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-11">[11]</a></sup></li>
</ul>
<dl>
<dd>
<ul>
<li>Combating <a href="/wiki/Gender_stereotypes" class="mw-redirect" title="Gender stereotypes">gender stereotypes</a> and sexism</li>
<li>Preventing and combating <a href="/wiki/Violence_against_women" title="Violence against women">violence against women</a></li>
<li>Guaranteeing Equal Access of Women to Justice</li>
<li>Achieving balanced participation of women and men in political and public decision-making</li>
<li>Achieving <a href="/wiki/Gender_mainstreaming" title="Gender mainstreaming">Gender Mainstreaming</a> in all policies and measures</li>
</ul>
</dd>
</dl>
<p>Such <a href="/wiki/Legislation" title="Legislation">legislation</a> and <a href="/wiki/Affirmative_action" title="Affirmative action">affirmative action</a> policies have been critical to bringing changes in societal attitudes. A 2015 Pew Research Center survey of citizens in 38 countries found that majorities in 37 of those 38 countries said that gender equality is at least "somewhat important," and a global median of 65% believe it is "very important" that women have the same rights as men.<sup id="cite_ref-12" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-12">[12]</a></sup> Most occupations are now equally available to men and women, in many countries. For example, many countries now permit women to serve in the <a href="/wiki/Armed_forces" title="Armed forces">armed forces</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Police" title="Police">police forces</a> and to be <a href="/wiki/Fire_fighter" class="mw-redirect" title="Fire fighter">fire fighters</a> – occupations traditionally reserved for men. Although these continue to have a male majority, an increasing number of women are now active, especially in directive fields such as politics, and occupy high positions in business.</p>
<p>Similarly, men are increasingly working in occupations which in previous generations had been considered <a href="/wiki/Women%27s_work" title="Women's work">women's work</a>, such as <a href="/wiki/Nursing" title="Nursing">nursing</a>, <a href="/wiki/Maid" title="Maid">cleaning</a> and <a href="/wiki/Child_care" title="Child care">child care</a>. In domestic situations, the role of <a href="/wiki/Parenting" title="Parenting">Parenting</a> or child rearing is more commonly shared or not as widely considered to be an exclusively female role, so that women may be free to pursue a <a href="/wiki/Career" title="Career">career</a> after <a href="/wiki/Childbirth" title="Childbirth">childbirth</a>. For further information, see <a href="/wiki/Shared_earning/shared_parenting_marriage" title="Shared earning/shared parenting marriage">Shared earning/shared parenting marriage</a>.</p>
<p>Another manifestation of the change in social attitudes is the <a href="/wiki/Married_and_maiden_names#Feminism_and_preserving_one.27s_personal_name" class="mw-redirect" title="Married and maiden names">non-automatic taking by a woman of her husband's surname on marriage</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-13" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-13">[13]</a></sup></p>
<p>A highly contentious issue relating to gender equality is the role of women in <a href="/wiki/Religious_law" title="Religious law">religiously orientated societies</a>. For example, <a href="/wiki/Cairo_Declaration_on_Human_Rights_in_Islam#Contents" title="Cairo Declaration on Human Rights in Islam">the Cairo Declaration on Human Rights in Islam</a> declared that women have equal dignity but not equal rights, and this was accepted by many predominantly Muslim countries. In some <a href="/wiki/Christianity" title="Christianity">Christian churches</a>, the practice of <a href="/wiki/Churching_of_women" title="Churching of women">churching of women</a> may still have elements of <a href="/wiki/Ritual_purification" title="Ritual purification">ritual purification</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Ordination_of_women" title="Ordination of women">Ordination of women</a> to the priesthood may be restricted or forbidden. Some Christians or Muslims believe in <a href="/wiki/Complementarianism" title="Complementarianism">Complementarianism</a>, a view that holds that men and women have different but complementing roles. This view may be in opposition to the views and goals of gender equality.</p>
<p>In addition, there are also non-Western countries of low religiosity where the contention surrounding gender equality remains. In China, a <a href="/wiki/Sex-selective_abortion_and_female_infanticide#Cultural_preference" class="mw-redirect" title="Sex-selective abortion and female infanticide">cultural preference for a male child</a> has resulted in a <a href="/wiki/Missing_women_of_Asia" class="mw-redirect" title="Missing women of Asia">shortfall of women</a> in the population. <a href="/wiki/Feminism_in_Japan" title="Feminism in Japan">The feminist movement in Japan</a> has made many strides which resulted in Rethe <a href="/wiki/Gender_Equality_Bureau" title="Gender Equality Bureau">Gender Equality Bureau</a>, but Japan still remains low in gender equality compared to other industrialized nations.</p>
<p>The notion of gender equality, and of its degree of achievement in a certain country, is very complex because there are countries that have a history of a high level of gender equality in <i>certain</i> areas of life but not in other areas. An example is <a href="/wiki/Finland" title="Finland">Finland</a>, which has offered very high opportunities to women in public/professional life but has had a weak legal approach to the issue of <a href="/wiki/Violence_against_women" title="Violence against women">violence against women</a>, with the situation in this country having been called a <a href="/wiki/Paradox" title="Paradox">paradox</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-14" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-14">[14]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-15" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-15">[15]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Amnesty_Finland_16-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Amnesty_Finland-16">[16]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Denmark" title="Denmark">Denmark</a> received harsh criticism for inadequate laws in regard to <a href="/wiki/Sexual_violence" title="Sexual violence">sexual violence</a> in a 2008 report produced by <a href="/wiki/Amnesty_International" title="Amnesty International">Amnesty International</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-Amnesty_Finland_16-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Amnesty_Finland-16">[16]</a></sup> which described Danish laws as "inconsistent with international human rights standards".<sup id="cite_ref-17" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-17">[17]</a></sup> This led to Denmark reforming its sexual offenses legislation in 2013.<sup id="cite_ref-18" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-18">[18]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-19" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-19">[19]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-20" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-20">[20]</a></sup> Indeed, there is a need for caution when categorizing countries by the level of gender equality that they have achieved.<sup id="cite_ref-21" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-21">[21]</a></sup> According to Mala Htun and Laurel Weldon "gender policy is not one issue but many" and:<sup id="cite_ref-22" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-22">[22]</a></sup></p>
<dl>
<dd>"When <a href="/wiki/Costa_Rica" title="Costa Rica">Costa Rica</a> has a better maternity leave than the <a href="/wiki/United_States" title="United States">United States</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Latin_America" title="Latin America">Latin American</a> countries are quicker to adopt policies addressing violence against women than the <a href="/wiki/Nordic_countries" title="Nordic countries">Nordic countries</a>, one at least ought to consider the possibility that fresh ways of grouping states would further the study of gender politics."</dd>
</dl>
<p>Not all beliefs relating to gender equality have been popularly adopted. For example <a href="/wiki/Topfreedom" title="Topfreedom">topfreedom</a>, the right to be bare breasted in public, frequently applies only to males and has remained a marginal issue. <a href="/wiki/Breastfeeding_in_public" title="Breastfeeding in public">Breastfeeding in public</a> is now more commonly tolerated, especially in semi-private places such as restaurants.<sup id="cite_ref-23" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-23">[23]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Gender_biases">Gender biases</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Gender_equality&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Gender biases">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>There has been criticism from some feminists towards the political discourse and policies employed in order to achieve the above items of "progress" in gender equality, with critics arguing that these gender equality strategies are superficial, in that they do not seek to challenge social structures of male domination, and only aim at improving the situation of women within the societal framework of subordination of women to men,<sup id="cite_ref-24" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-24">[24]</a></sup> and that official public policies (such as sate policies or international bodies policies) are questionable, as they are applied in a patriarchal context, and are directly or indirectly controlled by agents of a system which is for the most part male.<sup id="cite_ref-25" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-25">[25]</a></sup> One of the criticisms of the gender equality policies, in particular, those of the <a href="/wiki/European_Union" title="European Union">European Union</a>, is that they disproportionately focus on policies integrating women in public life, but do not seek to genuinely address the deep private sphere oppression.<sup id="cite_ref-26" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-26">[26]</a></sup></p>
<p>A further criticism is that a focus on the situation of women in non-Western countries, while often ignoring the issues that exist in the West, is a form of <a href="/wiki/Imperialism" title="Imperialism">imperialism</a> and of reinforcing Western moral superiority; and a way of "othering" of domestic violence, by presenting it as something specific to outsiders - the "violent others" - and not to the allegedly progressive Western cultures.<sup id="cite_ref-27" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-27">[27]</a></sup> These critics point out that women in Western countries often face similar problems, such as domestic violence and rape, as in other parts of the world.<sup id="cite_ref-28" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-28">[28]</a></sup> They also cite the fact that women faced de jure legal discrimination until just a few decades ago; for instance, in some Western countries such as Switzerland, Greece, Spain, and France, women obtained equal rights in <a href="/wiki/Family_law" title="Family law">family law</a> in the 1980s.<sup id="cite_ref-29" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-29">[29]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-30" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-30">[30]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-31" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-31">[31]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-32" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-32">[32]</a></sup> Another criticism is that there is a selective public discourse with regard to different types of oppression of women, with some forms of violence such as <a href="/wiki/Honor_killings" class="mw-redirect" title="Honor killings">honor killings</a> (most common in certain geographic regions such as parts of Asia and North Africa) being frequently the object of public debate, while other forms of violence, such as the lenient punishment for <a href="/wiki/Crimes_of_passion" class="mw-redirect" title="Crimes of passion">crimes of passion</a> across <a href="/wiki/Latin_America" title="Latin America">Latin America</a>, do not receive the same attention in the West.<sup id="cite_ref-National_33-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-National-33">[33]</a></sup> In 2002, Widney Brown, advocacy director for <a href="/wiki/Human_Rights_Watch" title="Human Rights Watch">Human Rights Watch</a>, pointed out that "crimes of passion have a similar dynamic [to honor killings] in that the women are killed by male family members and the crimes are perceived [in those relevant parts of the world] as excusable or understandable".<sup id="cite_ref-National_33-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-National-33">[33]</a></sup> It is also argued that the criticism of particular laws of many developing countries ignores the influence of <a href="/wiki/Colonialism" title="Colonialism">colonialism</a> on those legal systems, especially of the French <a href="/wiki/Napoleonic_Code" title="Napoleonic Code">Napoleonic Code</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-34" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-34">[34]</a></sup> which was extremely powerful in its influence over the world (historian Robert Holtman regards it as one of the few documents that have influenced the whole world<sup id="cite_ref-Robert_B._Holtman_1981_35-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Robert_B._Holtman_1981-35">[35]</a></sup>) and which designated married women a subordinate role, and provided for leniency with regard to 'crimes of passion' (which was the case in France until 1975<sup id="cite_ref-36" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-36">[36]</a></sup>).</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Efforts_to_fight_inequality">Efforts to fight inequality</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Gender_equality&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Efforts to fight inequality">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<table class="plainlinks metadata ambox ambox-content" role="presentation">
<tr>
<td class="mbox-image">
<div style="width:52px"><a href="/wiki/File:Crystal_Clear_app_kedit.svg" class="image"><img alt="Crystal Clear app kedit.svg" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e8/Crystal_Clear_app_kedit.svg/40px-Crystal_Clear_app_kedit.svg.png" width="40" height="40" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e8/Crystal_Clear_app_kedit.svg/60px-Crystal_Clear_app_kedit.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e8/Crystal_Clear_app_kedit.svg/80px-Crystal_Clear_app_kedit.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="128" data-file-height="128" /></a></div>
</td>
<td class="mbox-text"><span class="mbox-text-span">This section may need to be <b>rewritten entirely</b> to comply with Wikipedia's <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Manual_of_Style" title="Wikipedia:Manual of Style">quality standards</a>. <span class="hide-when-compact"><a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Gender_equality&amp;action=edit">You can help</a>. The <a href="/wiki/Talk:Gender_equality" title="Talk:Gender equality">discussion page</a> may contain suggestions.</span> <small><i>(June 2013)</i></small></span></td>
</tr>
</table>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">See also: <a href="/wiki/Gender_inequality" title="Gender inequality">Gender inequality</a></div>
<p>World bodies have defined gender equality in terms of <a href="/wiki/Human_rights" title="Human rights">human rights</a>, especially <a href="/wiki/Women%27s_rights" title="Women's rights">women's rights</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Economic_development" title="Economic development">economic development</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Smart_37-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Smart-37">[37]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-MDG3_38-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-MDG3-38">[38]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/UNICEF" title="UNICEF">UNICEF</a> describes that gender equality "means that women and men, and girls and boys, enjoy the same rights, resources, opportunities and protections. It does not require that girls and boys, or women and men, be the same, or that they be treated exactly alike."<sup id="cite_ref-UNICEF_39-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-UNICEF-39">[39]</a></sup></p>
<p><a href="/wiki/UNFPA" class="mw-redirect" title="UNFPA">UNFPA</a> stated that, "despite many international agreements affirming their human rights, women are still much more likely than men to be poor and illiterate. They have less access to property ownership, credit, training and employment. They are far less likely than men to be politically active and far more likely to be victims of domestic violence."<sup id="cite_ref-unfpa.org1_40-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-unfpa.org1-40">[40]</a></sup></p>
<p>Thus, promoting gender equality is seen as an encouragement to greater <a href="/wiki/Prosperity" title="Prosperity">economic prosperity</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Smart_37-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Smart-37">[37]</a></sup> For example, nations of the <a href="/wiki/Arab_world" title="Arab world">Arab world</a> that deny equality of opportunity to women were warned in a 2008 <a href="/wiki/United_Nations" title="United Nations">United Nations</a>-sponsored report that this disempowerment is a critical factor crippling these nations' return to the first rank of global leaders in commerce, <a href="/wiki/Education" title="Education">learning</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Culture" title="Culture">culture</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-ejoussour_41-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ejoussour-41">[41]</a></sup> That is, Western bodies are less likely to conduct commerce with nations in the Middle East that retain culturally accepted attitudes towards the status and function of women in their society in an effort to <a href="/wiki/Cultural_globalization" title="Cultural globalization">force them to change their beliefs</a> in the face of relatively underdeveloped economies.</p>
<p>In 2010, the <a href="/wiki/European_Union" title="European Union">European Union</a> opened the <a href="/wiki/European_Institute_for_Gender_Equality" title="European Institute for Gender Equality">European Institute for Gender Equality</a> (EIGE) in <a href="/wiki/Vilnius" title="Vilnius">Vilnius</a>, <a href="/wiki/Lithuania" title="Lithuania">Lithuania</a> to promote gender equality and to fight <a href="/wiki/Sex_discrimination" class="mw-redirect" title="Sex discrimination">sex discrimination</a>.</p>
<p>Gender equality is part of the national curriculum in Great Britain and many other European countries. <a href="/wiki/Personal,_Social_and_Health_Education" title="Personal, Social and Health Education">Personal, Social and Health Education</a>, <a href="/wiki/Religious_education" title="Religious education">religious studies</a> and <a href="/wiki/Language_acquisition" title="Language acquisition">Language acquisition</a> curricula tend to address gender equality issues as a very serious topic for discussion and analysis of its effect in society.</p>
<p>A large and growing body of research has shown how gender inequality undermines health and development. To overcome gender inequality the <a href="/wiki/United_Nations_Population_Fund" title="United Nations Population Fund">United Nations Population Fund</a> states that, "Women's empowerment and gender equality requires strategic interventions at all levels of programming and policy-making. These levels include reproductive health, economic empowerment, educational empowerment and political empowerment."<sup id="cite_ref-UNFPA_42-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-UNFPA-42">[42]</a></sup></p>
<p><a href="/wiki/UNFPA" class="mw-redirect" title="UNFPA">UNFPA</a> says that "research has also demonstrated how working with men and boys as well as women and girls to promote gender equality contributes to achieving health and development outcomes."<sup id="cite_ref-UNFPA_42-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-UNFPA-42">[42]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Violence_against_women">Violence against women</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Gender_equality&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Violence against women">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Acid_attack_victim.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/04/Acid_attack_victim.jpg/250px-Acid_attack_victim.jpg" width="250" height="166" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/04/Acid_attack_victim.jpg/375px-Acid_attack_victim.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/04/Acid_attack_victim.jpg/500px-Acid_attack_victim.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1024" data-file-height="680" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Acid_attack_victim.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Acid_attack" class="mw-redirect" title="Acid attack">Acid attack</a> victim in <a href="/wiki/Cambodia" title="Cambodia">Cambodia</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;"><a href="/wiki/File:FGM_road_sign,_Bakau,_Gambia,_2005.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b6/FGM_road_sign%2C_Bakau%2C_Gambia%2C_2005.jpg/250px-FGM_road_sign%2C_Bakau%2C_Gambia%2C_2005.jpg" width="250" height="208" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b6/FGM_road_sign%2C_Bakau%2C_Gambia%2C_2005.jpg/375px-FGM_road_sign%2C_Bakau%2C_Gambia%2C_2005.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b6/FGM_road_sign%2C_Bakau%2C_Gambia%2C_2005.jpg/500px-FGM_road_sign%2C_Bakau%2C_Gambia%2C_2005.jpg 2x" data-file-width="666" data-file-height="553" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:FGM_road_sign,_Bakau,_Gambia,_2005.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Anti-<a href="/wiki/FGM" class="mw-redirect" title="FGM">FGM</a> road sign, Bakau, Gambia, 2005</div>
</div>
</div>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Violence_against_women" title="Violence against women">Violence against women</a></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Violence_against_women" title="Violence against women">Violence against women</a> is a technical term used to collectively refer to violent acts that are primarily or exclusively committed against women. This type of violence is gender-based, meaning that the acts of violence are committed against women expressly <i>because</i> they are women, or as a result of patriarchal gender constructs. The UN <a href="/wiki/Declaration_on_the_Elimination_of_Violence_Against_Women" title="Declaration on the Elimination of Violence Against Women">Declaration on the Elimination of Violence Against Women</a> defines violence against women as "<i>any act of gender-based violence that results in, or is likely to result in, physical, sexual or psychological harm or suffering to women, including threats of such acts, coercion or arbitrary deprivation of liberty, whether occurring in public or in private life</i>" and states that:<sup id="cite_ref-43" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-43">[43]</a></sup></p>
<dl>
<dd>"violence against women is a manifestation of historically unequal power relations between men and women, which have led to domination over and discrimination against women by men and to the prevention of the full advancement of women, and that violence against women is one of the crucial social mechanisms by which women are forced into a subordinate position compared with men"</dd>
</dl>
<p>According to some theories, violence against women is often caused by the acceptance of violence by various cultural groups as a means of conflict resolution within intimate relationships. Studies on IPV victimization among ethnic minorities in the United Studies have consistently revealed that immigrants are a high-risk group for intimate violence.<sup id="cite_ref-44" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-44">[44]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-45" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-45">[45]</a></sup></p>
<p>Forms of violence against women include <a href="/wiki/Sexual_violence" title="Sexual violence">sexual violence</a> (including <a href="/wiki/War_rape" class="mw-redirect" title="War rape">war rape</a>, <a href="/wiki/Marital_rape" title="Marital rape">marital rape</a>, <a href="/w/index.php?title=Date_rape_by_drugs_or_alcohol&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Date rape by drugs or alcohol (page does not exist)">date rape by drugs or alcohol</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Child_sexual_abuse" title="Child sexual abuse">child sexual abuse</a>, the latter often in the context of <a href="/wiki/Child_marriage" title="Child marriage">child marriage</a>), <a href="/wiki/Domestic_violence" title="Domestic violence">domestic violence</a>, <a href="/wiki/Forced_marriage" title="Forced marriage">forced marriage</a>, <a href="/wiki/Female_genital_mutilation" title="Female genital mutilation">female genital mutilation</a>, <a href="/wiki/Forced_prostitution" title="Forced prostitution">forced prostitution</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sex_trafficking" title="Sex trafficking">sex trafficking</a>, <a href="/wiki/Honor_killing" title="Honor killing">honor killings</a>, <a href="/wiki/Dowry_killing" class="mw-redirect" title="Dowry killing">dowry killings</a>, <a href="/wiki/Acid_attacks" class="mw-redirect" title="Acid attacks">acid attacks</a>, <a href="/wiki/Stoning" title="Stoning">stoning</a>, <a href="/wiki/Flogging" class="mw-redirect" title="Flogging">flogging</a>, <a href="/wiki/Forced_sterilization" class="mw-redirect" title="Forced sterilization">forced sterilization</a>, <a href="/wiki/Forced_abortion" title="Forced abortion">forced abortion</a>, violence related to accusations of <a href="/wiki/Witchcraft" title="Witchcraft">witchcraft</a>, mistreatment of widows (e.g. <a href="/wiki/Widow_inheritance" title="Widow inheritance">widow inheritance</a>). Fighting against violence against women is considered a key issues for achieving gender equality. The Council of Europe adopted the <a href="/wiki/Convention_on_preventing_and_combating_violence_against_women_and_domestic_violence" title="Convention on preventing and combating violence against women and domestic violence">Convention on preventing and combating violence against women and domestic violence</a> (Istanbul Convention).</p>
<p>In Western countries, which are believed to be overall safe (i.e. where gang murders, armed kidnappings, civil unrest, and other similar acts are rare), the vast majority of murdered women are killed by partners/ex-partners: as of 2004-2009, former and current partners were responsible for more than 80% of all cases of <a href="/wiki/Femicide" title="Femicide">murders of women</a> in <a href="/wiki/Cyprus" title="Cyprus">Cyprus</a>, <a href="/wiki/France" title="France">France</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Portugal" title="Portugal">Portugal</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-smallarmssurvey.org_46-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-smallarmssurvey.org-46">[46]</a></sup> By contrast, in countries with a high level of organized criminal activity and gang violence murders of women are more likely to occur in a public sphere, often in a general climate of indifference and impunity.<sup id="cite_ref-smallarmssurvey.org_46-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-smallarmssurvey.org-46">[46]</a></sup> In addition, many countries do not have adequate comprehensive data collection on such murders, aggravating the problem.<sup id="cite_ref-smallarmssurvey.org_46-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-smallarmssurvey.org-46">[46]</a></sup></p>
<p>In some parts of the world, various forms of violence against women are tolerated and accepted as parts of everyday life; according to UNFPA:<sup id="cite_ref-47" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-47">[47]</a></sup></p>
<dl>
<dd>"In some developing countries, practices that subjugate and harm women - such as wife-beating, killings in the name of honour, female genital mutilation/cutting and dowry deaths - are condoned as being part of the natural order of things."</dd>
</dl>
<p>In most countries, it is only in more recent decades that violence against women (in particular when committed in the family) has received significant legal attention. The <a href="/wiki/Convention_on_preventing_and_combating_violence_against_women_and_domestic_violence" title="Convention on preventing and combating violence against women and domestic violence">Istanbul Convention</a> acknowledges the long tradition of European countries of ignoring, <i>de jure</i> or <i>de facto</i>, this form of violence. In its <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://conventions.coe.int/Treaty/EN/Reports/Html/210.htm">explanatory report</a> at para 219, it states:</p>
<dl>
<dd>"There are many examples from past practice in Council of Europe member states that show that exceptions to the prosecution of such cases were made, either in law or in practice, if victim and perpetrator were, for example, married to each other or had been in a relationship. The most prominent example is rape within marriage, which for a long time had not been recognised as rape because of the relationship between victim and perpetrator."<sup id="cite_ref-48" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-48">[48]</a></sup></dd>
</dl>
<p>In <i>Opuz v Turkey</i>, the <a href="/wiki/European_Court_of_Human_Rights" title="European Court of Human Rights">European Court of Human Rights</a> recognized violence against women as a form discrimination against women,<sup id="cite_ref-49" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-49">[49]</a></sup> para 200: "[T]he Court considers that the violence suffered by the applicant and her mother may be regarded as <i>gender-based violence which is a form of discrimination against women</i>."<sup id="cite_ref-50" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-50">[50]</a></sup> This is also the position of the Istanbul Convention which reads:</p>
<dl>
<dd>"Article 3 – Definitions, For the purpose of this Convention: a "violence against women" is understood as a violation of human rights and <i>a form of discrimination against women</i> [...]".<sup id="cite_ref-51" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-51">[51]</a></sup></dd>
</dl>
<p>In some cultures, acts of violence against women are seen as crimes against the male 'owners' of the woman, such as husband, father or male relatives, rather the woman herself. This leads to practices where men inflict violence upon women in order to get revenge on male members of the women's family. Such practices include <a href="/wiki/Types_of_rape#Payback_rape" title="Types of rape">payback rape</a>, a form of rape specific to certain cultures, particularly the <a href="/wiki/Pacific_Islands" title="Pacific Islands">Pacific Islands</a>, which consists of the rape of a female, usually by a group of several males, as revenge for acts committed by members of her family, such as her father or brothers, with the rape being meant to humiliate the father or brothers, as punishment for their prior behavior towards the perpetrators.<sup id="cite_ref-ReferenceB_52-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ReferenceB-52">[52]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-53" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-53">[53]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Reproductive_and_sexual_health_and_rights">Reproductive and sexual health and rights</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Gender_equality&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Reproductive and sexual health and rights">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Reproductive_health" title="Reproductive health">Reproductive health</a> and <a href="/wiki/Reproductive_rights" title="Reproductive rights">Reproductive rights</a></div>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Forced_sterilization" class="mw-redirect" title="Forced sterilization">Forced sterilization</a>, <a href="/wiki/Forced_pregnancy" title="Forced pregnancy">Forced pregnancy</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Forced_abortion" title="Forced abortion">Forced abortion</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Maternal_mortality_rate_worldwide.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2c/Maternal_mortality_rate_worldwide.jpg/220px-Maternal_mortality_rate_worldwide.jpg" width="220" height="100" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2c/Maternal_mortality_rate_worldwide.jpg/330px-Maternal_mortality_rate_worldwide.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2c/Maternal_mortality_rate_worldwide.jpg/440px-Maternal_mortality_rate_worldwide.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1800" data-file-height="820" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Maternal_mortality_rate_worldwide.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Global maternal mortality rate per 100 000 live births, (2010)<sup id="cite_ref-CIA_54-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CIA-54">[54]</a></sup></div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Maternal_health_(4798750001).jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/15/Maternal_health_%284798750001%29.jpg/220px-Maternal_health_%284798750001%29.jpg" width="220" height="293" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/15/Maternal_health_%284798750001%29.jpg/330px-Maternal_health_%284798750001%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/15/Maternal_health_%284798750001%29.jpg/440px-Maternal_health_%284798750001%29.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2304" data-file-height="3072" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Maternal_health_(4798750001).jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
In 2010, <a href="/wiki/Sierra_Leone" title="Sierra Leone">Sierra Leone</a> launched free healthcare for pregnant and breastfeeding women</div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Guangzhou-family-planning-posters-0532.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ea/Guangzhou-family-planning-posters-0532.jpg/220px-Guangzhou-family-planning-posters-0532.jpg" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ea/Guangzhou-family-planning-posters-0532.jpg/330px-Guangzhou-family-planning-posters-0532.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ea/Guangzhou-family-planning-posters-0532.jpg/440px-Guangzhou-family-planning-posters-0532.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2048" data-file-height="1536" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Guangzhou-family-planning-posters-0532.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
A wall along a residential lane in <a href="/wiki/Guangzhou" title="Guangzhou">Guangzhou</a>, <a href="/wiki/China" title="China">China</a> with family planning posters stressing the importance of balanced sex-ratios, in order to prevent <a href="/wiki/Sex-selective_abortion" title="Sex-selective abortion">sex-selective abortion</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The importance of women having the right and possibility to have control over their body, reproduction decisions, and sexuality, and the need for gender equality in order to achieve these goals are recognized as crucial by the <a href="/wiki/Fourth_World_Conference_on_Women" title="Fourth World Conference on Women">Fourth World Conference on Women</a> in Beijing and the UN <a href="/wiki/International_Conference_on_Population_and_Development" title="International Conference on Population and Development">International Conference on Population and Development</a> Program of Action. The World Health Organization (WHO) has stated that promotion of gender equality is crucial in the fight against <a href="/wiki/HIV/AIDS" title="HIV/AIDS">HIV/AIDS</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-ReferenceA_55-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ReferenceA-55">[55]</a></sup></p>
<p><a href="/wiki/Maternal_mortality" class="mw-redirect" title="Maternal mortality">Maternal mortality</a> is a major problem in many parts of the world. <a href="/wiki/UNFPA" class="mw-redirect" title="UNFPA">UNFPA</a> states that countries have an obligation to protect women's right to health, but many countries do not do that.<sup id="cite_ref-unfpa.org_56-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-unfpa.org-56">[56]</a></sup> Maternal mortality is considered today not just an issue of development but also an issue of <a href="/wiki/Human_rights" title="Human rights">human rights</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-unfpa.org_56-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-unfpa.org-56">[56]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/UNFPA" class="mw-redirect" title="UNFPA">UNFPA</a> says that, "since 1990, the world has seen a 45 per cent decline in maternal mortality – an enormous achievement. But in spite of these gains, almost 800 women still die every day from causes related to pregnancy or childbirth. This is about one woman every two minutes."<sup id="cite_ref-57" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-57">[57]</a></sup> According to UNFPA:<sup id="cite_ref-58" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-58">[58]</a></sup></p>
<dl>
<dd>"Preventable maternal mortality occurs where there is a failure to give effect to the rights of women to health, equality, and non-discrimination. Preventable maternal mortality also often represents a violation of a woman’s right to life."</dd>
</dl>
<p>The right to reproductive and sexual autonomy is denied to women in many parts of the world, through practices such as <a href="/wiki/Forced_sterilization" class="mw-redirect" title="Forced sterilization">forced sterilization</a>, forced/coerced sexual partnering (e.g. <a href="/wiki/Forced_marriage" title="Forced marriage">forced marriage</a>, <a href="/wiki/Child_marriage" title="Child marriage">child marriage</a>), criminalization of consensual sexual acts (such as <a href="/wiki/Sex_outside_marriage" class="mw-redirect" title="Sex outside marriage">sex outside marriage</a>), lack of criminalization of <a href="/wiki/Marital_rape" title="Marital rape">marital rape</a>, violence in regard to the choice of partner (<a href="/wiki/Honor_killings" class="mw-redirect" title="Honor killings">honor killings</a> as punishment for 'inappropriate' relations). Amnesty International’s Secretary General has stated that: "It is unbelievable that in the twenty-first century some countries are condoning child marriage and marital rape while others are outlawing abortion, sex outside marriage and same-sex sexual activity – even punishable by death."<sup id="cite_ref-59" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-59">[59]</a></sup> These practices infringe on the right of achieving reproductive and sexual health. High Commissioner for Human Rights <a href="/wiki/Navi_Pillay" title="Navi Pillay">Navi Pillay</a> has called for full respect and recognition of women's autonomy and sexual and reproductive health rights, stating:<sup id="cite_ref-60" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-60">[60]</a></sup></p>
<dl>
<dd>"Violations of women's human rights are often linked to their sexuality and reproductive role. Women are frequently treated as property, they are sold into marriage, into trafficking, into sexual slavery. Violence against women frequently takes the form of sexual violence. Victims of such violence are often accused of promiscuity and held responsible for their fate, while infertile women are rejected by husbands, families, and communities. In many countries, married women may not refuse to have sexual relations with their husbands, and often have no say in whether they use contraception."</dd>
</dl>
<p><a href="/wiki/Adolescent" class="mw-redirect" title="Adolescent">Adolescent</a> girls have the highest risk of sexual coercion, sexual ill health, and negative reproductive outcomes. The risks they face are higher than those of boys and men; this increased risk is partly due to gender inequity (different socialization of boys and girls, gender based violence, child marriage) and partly due to biological factors (females' risk of acquiring sexually transmitted infections during unprotected sexual relations is two to four times that of males').<sup id="cite_ref-61" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-61">[61]</a></sup></p>
<p>Socialization within rigid gender constructs often creates an environment where sexual violence is common; according to the WHO: "Sexual violence is also more likely to occur where beliefs in male sexual entitlement are strong, where gender roles are more rigid, and in countries experiencing high rates of other types of violence."<sup id="cite_ref-62" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-62">[62]</a></sup> The sexual health of women is often poor in societies where a woman's right to control her sexuality is not recognized. <a href="/wiki/Richard_A._Posner" class="mw-redirect" title="Richard A. Posner">Richard A. Posner</a> writes that "Traditionally, rape was the offense of depriving a father or husband of a valuable asset — his wife's chastity or his daughter's virginity".<sup id="cite_ref-63" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-63">[63]</a></sup> Historically, rape was seen in many cultures (and is still seen today in some societies) as a crime against the <a href="/wiki/Family_honor" title="Family honor">honor of the family</a>, rather than against the self-determination of the woman. As a result, victims of rape may face violence, in extreme cases even honor killings, at the hands of their family members.<sup id="cite_ref-64" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-64">[64]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-65" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-65">[65]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Catharine_MacKinnon" title="Catharine MacKinnon">Catharine MacKinnon</a> argues that in male dominated societies, sexual intercourse is imposed on women in a coercive and unequal way, creating a continuum of victimization, where women have few positive sexual experiences; she writes "To know what is wrong with rape, know what is right about sex. If this, in turn, is difficult, the difficulty is as instructive as the difficulty men have in telling the difference when women see one. Perhaps the wrong of rape has proved so difficult to define because the unquestionable starting point has been that rape is defined as distinct from intercourse, while for women it is difficult to distinguish the two under conditions of male dominance."<sup id="cite_ref-66" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-66">[66]</a></sup></p>
<p>One of the challenges of dealing with sexual violence is that in many societies women are perceived as being readily available for sex, and men are seen as entitled to their bodies, until and unless women object. Rebecca Cook wrote in <i>Submission of Interights to the European Court of Human Rights in the case of <a href="/wiki/M.C._v._Bulgaria" class="mw-redirect" title="M.C. v. Bulgaria">M.C. v. Bulgaria</a>, 12 April 2003</i>:<sup id="cite_ref-67" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-67">[67]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-68" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-68">[68]</a></sup></p>
<dl>
<dd>"The equality approach starts by examining not whether the woman said 'no', but whether she said 'yes'. Women do not walk around in a state of constant consent to sexual activity unless and until they say 'no', or offer resistance to anyone who targets them for sexual activity. The right to physical and sexual autonomy means that they have to affirmatively consent to sexual activity."</dd>
</dl>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Freedom_of_movement">Freedom of movement</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Gender_equality&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Freedom of movement">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Group_of_Women_Wearing_Burkas.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b2/Group_of_Women_Wearing_Burkas.jpg/220px-Group_of_Women_Wearing_Burkas.jpg" width="220" height="144" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b2/Group_of_Women_Wearing_Burkas.jpg/330px-Group_of_Women_Wearing_Burkas.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b2/Group_of_Women_Wearing_Burkas.jpg/440px-Group_of_Women_Wearing_Burkas.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1536" data-file-height="1002" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Group_of_Women_Wearing_Burkas.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Women in <a href="/wiki/Afghanistan" title="Afghanistan">Afghanistan</a> wearing <a href="/wiki/Burqa" title="Burqa">burqas</a>. Some clothes that women are required, by law or custom, to wear, can restrict their movements</div>
</div>
</div>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Freedom_of_movement" title="Freedom of movement">Freedom of movement</a></div>
<p>The degree to which women can participate (in law and in practice) in public life varies by culture, historical era, social class and other other socioeconomic characteristics. <a href="/wiki/Seclusion" title="Seclusion">Seclusion</a> of women within the home was a common practice among the <a href="/wiki/Upper_classes" class="mw-redirect" title="Upper classes">upper classes</a> of many societies, and this still remains the case today in some societies. Before the 20th century it was also common in parts of Southern Europe, such as much of Spain. <sup id="cite_ref-69" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-69">[69]</a></sup></p>
<p>Women's <a href="/wiki/Freedom_of_movement" title="Freedom of movement">freedom of movement</a> continues to be legally restricted in some parts of the world. This restriction is often due to <a href="/wiki/Marriage_laws" class="mw-redirect" title="Marriage laws">marriage laws</a>. For instance, in <a href="/wiki/Yemen" title="Yemen">Yemen</a>, marriage regulations stipulate that a wife must obey her husband and must not leave home without his permission.<sup id="cite_ref-www2.ohchr.org_70-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-www2.ohchr.org-70">[70]</a></sup> In some countries, women must legally be accompanied by their male guardians (such as the husband or male relative) when they leave home.<sup id="cite_ref-71" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-71">[71]</a></sup></p>
<p>The CEDAW states at Article 15 (4) that:<sup id="cite_ref-72" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-72">[72]</a></sup></p>
<dl>
<dd>Article 15</dd>
<dd>"4. States Parties shall accord to men and women the same rights with regard to the law relating to the movement of persons and the freedom to choose their residence and domicile."</dd>
</dl>
<p>In addition to laws, women's freedom of movement is also restricted by social and religious norms - for example, <a href="/wiki/Purdah" title="Purdah">purdah</a>, a religious and social practice of female seclusion prevalent among some Muslim communities in <a href="/wiki/Afghanistan" title="Afghanistan">Afghanistan</a> and <a href="/wiki/Pakistan" title="Pakistan">Pakistan</a> as well as upper-caste Hindus in <a href="/wiki/North_India" title="North India">Northern India</a>, such as the <a href="/wiki/Rajputs" class="mw-redirect" title="Rajputs">Rajputs</a>, which often leads to the minimizing of the movement of women in public spaces and restrictions on their social and professional interactions;<sup id="cite_ref-Papanek_73-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Papanek-73">[73]</a></sup> or <a href="/wiki/Namus" title="Namus">namus</a>, a cultural concept strongly related to <a href="/wiki/Family_honor" title="Family honor">family honor</a>.</p>
<p>The custom of <a href="/wiki/Bride_price" title="Bride price">bride price</a> can also curtail the free movement of women: if a wife wants to leave her husband, he may demand back the bride price that he had paid to the woman's family; and the woman's family often cannot or does not want to pay it back, making it difficult for women to move out of violent husbands' homes.<sup id="cite_ref-74" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-74">[74]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-75" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-75">[75]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-76" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-76">[76]</a></sup></p>
<p>Restrictions on freedom of movement also exist due to traditional practices such as <a href="/wiki/Baad_(practice)" title="Baad (practice)">baad</a>, <a href="/wiki/Swara_(custom)" title="Swara (custom)">swara</a>, or <a href="/wiki/Vani_(custom)" title="Vani (custom)">vani</a>, common especially among <a href="/wiki/Pashtun_tribes" title="Pashtun tribes">Pashtun tribes</a> in Pakistan and Afghanistan, whereby a girl is given from one family to another (often though a marriage), in order to settle the disputes and feuds between the families. The girl, who now belongs to the second family, has very little autonomy and freedom, her role being to serve the new family.<sup id="cite_ref-77" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-77">[77]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-78" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-78">[78]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-79" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-79">[79]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-hk2004_80-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-hk2004-80">[80]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-81" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-81">[81]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Gendered_arrangements_of_work_and_care">Gendered arrangements of work and care</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Gender_equality&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Gendered arrangements of work and care">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Since the 1950s, social scientists as well as feminists have increasingly criticized gendered arrangements of work and care and the male breadwinner role. Policies are increasingly targeting men as fathers as a tool of changing gender relations.<sup id="cite_ref-82" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-82">[82]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Shared_earning/shared_parenting_marriage" title="Shared earning/shared parenting marriage">Shared earning/shared parenting marriage</a>, that is, a relationship where the partners collaborate at sharing their responsibilities inside and outside of the home, is often encouraged in Western countries.<sup id="cite_ref-Vachon_83-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Vachon-83">[83]</a></sup></p>
<p>Western countries with a strong emphasis on women fulfilling the role of homemakers, rather than a professional role, include parts of German speaking Europe - parts of Germany, Austria and Switzerland; as well as the Netherlands and Ireland. In 2011, <a href="/wiki/Jose_Manuel_Barroso" class="mw-redirect" title="Jose Manuel Barroso">Jose Manuel Barroso</a>, then president of the <a href="/wiki/European_Commission" title="European Commission">European Commission</a>, stated "Germany, but also Austria and the Netherlands, should look at the example of the northern countries [...] that means removing obstacles for women, older workers, foreigners and low-skilled job-seekers to get into the workforce".<sup id="cite_ref-84" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-84">[84]</a></sup> The Netherlands and Ireland are among the last Western countries to accept women as professionals; despite the Netherlands having an image as progressive on gender issues, women in the Netherlands work less in paid employment than women in other comparable Western countries. In the early 1980s, the Commission of the <a href="/wiki/European_Communities" title="European Communities">European Communities</a> report <i>Women in the European Community</i>, found that the Netherlands and Ireland had the lowest labour participation of married women and the most public disapproval of it.<sup id="cite_ref-85" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-85">[85]</a></sup> In Ireland, until 1973, there was a <a href="/wiki/Marriage_bar" title="Marriage bar">marriage bar</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-86" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-86">[86]</a></sup> In the Netherlands, from the 1990s onwards, the numbers of women entering the workplace have increased, but with most of the women <a href="/wiki/Part_time_job" class="mw-redirect" title="Part time job">working part time</a>. As of 2014, the Netherlands and Switzerland were the only OECD members where most employed women worked part-time,<sup id="cite_ref-87" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-87">[87]</a></sup> while in the United Kingdom, women made up two-thirds of workers on long term sick leave, despite making up only half of the workforce and even after excluding maternity leave.<sup id="cite_ref-88" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-88">[88]</a></sup></p>
<p>A key issue towards insuring gender equality in the workplace is the respecting of <a href="/wiki/Maternity_rights" class="mw-redirect" title="Maternity rights">maternity rights</a> and <a href="/wiki/Reproductive_rights" title="Reproductive rights">reproductive rights</a> of women.<sup id="cite_ref-89" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-89">[89]</a></sup> Different countries have different rules regarding <a href="/wiki/Maternity_leave" class="mw-redirect" title="Maternity leave">maternity leave</a>, paternity leave and parental leave. In the <a href="/wiki/European_Union" title="European Union">European Union</a> (EU) the policies vary significantly by country, but the EU members must abide by the minimum standards of the <a href="/wiki/Pregnant_Workers_Directive" class="mw-redirect" title="Pregnant Workers Directive">Pregnant Workers Directive</a> and <a href="/wiki/Parental_Leave_Directive" class="mw-redirect" title="Parental Leave Directive">Parental Leave Directive</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-90" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-90">[90]</a></sup> Another important issue refers to ensuring that employed women are not <i><a href="/wiki/De_jure" title="De jure">de jure</a></i> or <i><a href="/wiki/De_facto" title="De facto">de facto</a></i> prevented from having a child. For example, some countries have enacted legislation explicitly outlawing or restricting what they view as abusive clauses in <a href="/wiki/Employment_contract" title="Employment contract">employment contracts</a> regarding reproductive rights (for example clauses which stipulate that a woman cannot get pregnant during a specified time) rendering such contracts <a href="/wiki/Void_(law)" title="Void (law)">void</a> or <a href="/wiki/Voidable" title="Voidable">voidable</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-91" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-91">[91]</a></sup> In some countries, employers who request women to sign formal or informal documents stipulating that they will not get pregnant face legal punishment.<sup id="cite_ref-92" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-92">[92]</a></sup> Women often face severe violations of their reproductive rights at the hands of their employers; and the <a href="/wiki/International_Labour_Organization" title="International Labour Organization">International Labour Organization</a> classifies <a href="/wiki/Forced_abortion" title="Forced abortion">forced abortion</a> coerced by the employer as <a href="/wiki/Exploitation_of_labour" title="Exploitation of labour">labour exploitation</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-93" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-93">[93]</a></sup> Being the victim of a forced abortion compelled by the employer was ruled a ground of obtaining <a href="/wiki/Political_asylum" class="mw-redirect" title="Political asylum">political asylum</a> in the US.<sup id="cite_ref-94" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-94">[94]</a></sup> Other abuses include routine <a href="/wiki/Virginity_test" title="Virginity test">virginity tests</a> of unmarried employed women.<sup id="cite_ref-95" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-95">[95]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-96" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-96">[96]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Girls.27_access_to_education">Girls' access to education</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Gender_equality&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Girls' access to education">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Girls_lining_up_for_class_-_Flickr_-_Al_Jazeera_English.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a2/Girls_lining_up_for_class_-_Flickr_-_Al_Jazeera_English.jpg/220px-Girls_lining_up_for_class_-_Flickr_-_Al_Jazeera_English.jpg" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a2/Girls_lining_up_for_class_-_Flickr_-_Al_Jazeera_English.jpg/330px-Girls_lining_up_for_class_-_Flickr_-_Al_Jazeera_English.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a2/Girls_lining_up_for_class_-_Flickr_-_Al_Jazeera_English.jpg/440px-Girls_lining_up_for_class_-_Flickr_-_Al_Jazeera_English.jpg 2x" data-file-width="640" data-file-height="480" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Girls_lining_up_for_class_-_Flickr_-_Al_Jazeera_English.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
School girls in <a href="/wiki/Gaza_Strip" title="Gaza Strip">Gaza Strip</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Female_education" title="Female education">Female education</a> and <a href="/wiki/Gender_and_education" class="mw-redirect" title="Gender and education">Gender and education</a></div>
<p>In many parts of the world, girls' access to education is very restricted. In developing parts of the world women are often denied opportunities for education as girls and women face many obstacles. These include: early and forced marriages; early pregnancy; prejudice based on gender stereotypes at home, at school and in the community; violence on the way to school, or in and around schools; long distances to schools; vulnerability to the HIV epidemic; school fees, which often lead to parents sending only their sons to school; lack of gender sensitive approaches and materials in classrooms.<sup id="cite_ref-97" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-97">[97]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-98" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-98">[98]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-99" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-99">[99]</a></sup> According to <a href="/wiki/OHCHR" class="mw-redirect" title="OHCHR">OHCHR</a>, there have been multiple attacks on schools worldwide during the period 2009-2014 with "a number of these attacks being specifically directed at girls, parents and teachers advocating for gender equality in education".<sup id="cite_ref-100" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-100">[100]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/United_Nations_Population_Fund" title="United Nations Population Fund">United Nations Population Fund</a> says:<sup id="cite_ref-101" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-101">[101]</a></sup></p>
<dl>
<dd>"About two thirds of the world's <a href="/wiki/Literacy#Gender_disparities" title="Literacy">illiterate</a> adults are women. Lack of an education severely restricts a woman's access to information and opportunities. Conversely, increasing women's and girls' educational attainment benefits both individuals and future generations. Higher levels of women's education are strongly associated with lower infant mortality and lower fertility, as well as better outcomes for their children."</dd>
</dl>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Political_participation_of_women">Political participation of women</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Gender_equality&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Political participation of women">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Women_in_government" title="Women in government">Women in government</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Map3.8Government_Participation_by_Women_compressed.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/16/Map3.8Government_Participation_by_Women_compressed.jpg/220px-Map3.8Government_Participation_by_Women_compressed.jpg" width="220" height="138" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/16/Map3.8Government_Participation_by_Women_compressed.jpg/330px-Map3.8Government_Participation_by_Women_compressed.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/16/Map3.8Government_Participation_by_Women_compressed.jpg/440px-Map3.8Government_Participation_by_Women_compressed.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2402" data-file-height="1502" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
