<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Map3.8Government_Participation_by_Women_compressed.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
A world map showing countries governmental participation by women, 2010.</div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:National_Association_Against_Woman_Suffrage.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9b/National_Association_Against_Woman_Suffrage.jpg/220px-National_Association_Against_Woman_Suffrage.jpg" width="220" height="176" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9b/National_Association_Against_Woman_Suffrage.jpg/330px-National_Association_Against_Woman_Suffrage.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9b/National_Association_Against_Woman_Suffrage.jpg/440px-National_Association_Against_Woman_Suffrage.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1148" data-file-height="917" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:National_Association_Against_Woman_Suffrage.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Headquarters of the National Association Opposed to Woman Suffrage, United States, early 20th century</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Women are underrepresented in most countries' National Parliaments.<sup id="cite_ref-102" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-102">[102]</a></sup> The 2011 UN General Assembly resolution on women’s political participation called for female participation in politics, and expressed concern about the fact that "women in every part of the world continue to be largely marginalized from the political sphere".<sup id="cite_ref-103" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-103">[103]</a></sup> The Council of Europe states that:<sup id="cite_ref-coe.int_104-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-coe.int-104">[104]</a></sup></p>
<dl>
<dd>"Pluralist democracy requires balanced participation of women and men in political and public decision-making. Council of Europe standards provide clear guidance on how to achieve this."</dd>
</dl>
<p>Institutions also play an essential role in achieving and enforcing gender equality. However, basic legal and human rights, access to and the control of resources, employment and earnings and social and political participation are still not guaranteed in many social and legal institutions. For example, only 22 percent of parliamentarians globally are women and therefore, men continue to occupy most positions of political and legal authority.<sup id="cite_ref-unfpa.org1_40-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-unfpa.org1-40">[40]</a></sup> As of November 2014, women accounted for 28% of members of the single or lower houses of parliaments in the <a href="/wiki/European_Union" title="European Union">European Union</a> member states.<sup id="cite_ref-105" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-105">[105]</a></sup></p>
<p>In some Western countries women have only recently <a href="/wiki/Women%27s_suffrage" title="Women's suffrage">obtained the right to vote</a>, notably in <a href="/wiki/Switzerland" title="Switzerland">Switzerland</a>, where women gained the right to vote in federal elections in 1971;<sup id="cite_ref-switzerland-chronology_106-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-switzerland-chronology-106">[106]</a></sup> but in the canton of <a href="/wiki/Appenzell_Innerrhoden" title="Appenzell Innerrhoden">Appenzell Innerrhoden</a> women obtained the right to vote on local issues only in 1991, when the canton was forced to do so by the <a href="/wiki/Federal_Supreme_Court_of_Switzerland" title="Federal Supreme Court of Switzerland">Federal Supreme Court of Switzerland</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-107" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-107">[107]</a></sup> In <a href="/wiki/Liechtenstein" title="Liechtenstein">Liechtenstein</a>, women were given the right to vote by the <a href="/wiki/Liechtenstein_women%27s_suffrage_referendum,_1984" title="Liechtenstein women's suffrage referendum, 1984">women's suffrage referendum of 1984</a>. Three prior referendums held in <a href="/wiki/Liechtenstein_women%27s_suffrage_referendum,_1968" class="mw-redirect" title="Liechtenstein women's suffrage referendum, 1968">1968</a>, <a href="/wiki/Liechtenstein_women%27s_suffrage_referendum,_1971" title="Liechtenstein women's suffrage referendum, 1971">1971</a> and <a href="/wiki/Liechtenstein_women%27s_suffrage_referendum,_1973" class="mw-redirect" title="Liechtenstein women's suffrage referendum, 1973">1973</a> had failed to secure women's right to vote.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Economic_empowerment_of_women">Economic empowerment of women</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Gender_equality&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Economic empowerment of women">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Women_in_the_workforce" title="Women in the workforce">Women in the workforce</a>, <a href="/wiki/Female_economic_activity" title="Female economic activity">Female economic activity</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Gender-based_price_discrimination" title="Gender-based price discrimination">Gender-based price discrimination</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a href="/wiki/File:EU_27_Gender_Pay_Gap_2014.png" class="image"><img alt="Bar graph showing the gender pay gap in European countries" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/63/EU_27_Gender_Pay_Gap_2014.png/300px-EU_27_Gender_Pay_Gap_2014.png" width="300" height="169" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/63/EU_27_Gender_Pay_Gap_2014.png/450px-EU_27_Gender_Pay_Gap_2014.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/63/EU_27_Gender_Pay_Gap_2014.png/600px-EU_27_Gender_Pay_Gap_2014.png 2x" data-file-width="1280" data-file-height="720" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:EU_27_Gender_Pay_Gap_2014.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Gender_pay_gap" title="Gender pay gap">Gender pay gap</a> in average gross hourly earnings in the <a href="/wiki/European_Union" title="European Union">EU</a> member states, according to <a href="/wiki/Eurostat" title="Eurostat">Eurostat</a> 2014.<sup id="cite_ref-EC_stats_108-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-EC_stats-108">[108]</a></sup></div>
</div>
</div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Female_economic_activity" title="Female economic activity">Female economic activity</a> is a common measure of gender equality in an economy. <a href="/wiki/UN_Women" title="UN Women">UN Women</a> states that: "Investing in women’s economic empowerment sets a direct path towards gender equality, poverty eradication and inclusive economic growth."<sup id="cite_ref-unwomen.org_109-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-unwomen.org-109">[109]</a></sup></p>
<p>Gender discrimination often results in women obtaining low-wage jobs and being disproportionately affected by poverty, discrimination and exploitation.<sup id="cite_ref-unwomen.org_109-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-unwomen.org-109">[109]</a></sup></p>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/UN_Population_Fund" class="mw-redirect" title="UN Population Fund">UN Population Fund</a> says that, "Six out of 10 of the world’s poorest people are women. Economic disparities persist partly because much of the unpaid work within families and communities falls on the shoulders of women, and because women continue to face discrimination in the economic sphere."<sup id="cite_ref-unfpa.org1_40-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-unfpa.org1-40">[40]</a></sup></p>
<p>Gender biases also exist in product and service provision. <sup id="cite_ref-:0_110-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:0-110">[110]</a></sup> The term "Women's Tax", also known as "Pink Tax", refers to gendered pricing in which products or services marketed to women are more expensive than similar products marketed to men. <a href="/wiki/Gender-based_price_discrimination" title="Gender-based price discrimination">Gender-based price discrimination</a> involves companies selling almost identical units of the same product or service at comparatively different prices, as determined by the target market. Studies have found that women pay about $1,400 a year more than men due to gendered discriminatory pricing. Although the "pink tax" of different goods and services is not uniform, overall women pay more for commodities that result in visual evidence of feminine body image.<sup id="cite_ref-111" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-111">[111]</a></sup> For example, studies have shown that women are charged more for services especially tailoring, hair cutting and laundering.<sup id="cite_ref-:0_110-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:0-110">[110]</a></sup></p>
<p>A growing body of research documents what works to economically empower women, from providing access to formal financial services to training on agricultural and business management practices, though more research is needed across a variety of contexts to confirm the effectiveness of these interventions.<sup id="cite_ref-112" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-112">[112]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Marriage.2C_divorce_and_property_laws_and_regulations">Marriage, divorce and property laws and regulations</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Gender_equality&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Marriage, divorce and property laws and regulations">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Equal rights for women in marriage, divorce, and property/land ownership and inheritance are essential for gender equality. CEDAW has called for the end of discriminatory <a href="/wiki/Family_law" title="Family law">family laws</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-113" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-113">[113]</a></sup> In 2013, UN Women stated that "While at least 115 countries recognize equal land rights for women and men, effective implementation remains a major challenge".<sup id="cite_ref-114" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-114">[114]</a></sup></p>
<p>The legal and social treatment of married women has been often discussed as a political issue from the 19th century onwards. <a href="/wiki/John_Stuart_Mill" title="John Stuart Mill">John Stuart Mill</a>, in <i><a href="/wiki/The_Subjection_of_Women" title="The Subjection of Women">The Subjection of Women</a></i> (1869) compared marriage to <a href="/wiki/Slavery" title="Slavery">slavery</a> and wrote that: "The law of servitude in marriage is a monstrous contradiction to all the principles of the modern world, and to all the experience through which those principles have been slowly and painfully worked out."<sup id="cite_ref-115" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-115">[115]</a></sup> In 1957, James Everett, then Minister for Justice in Ireland, stated: "The progress of organised society is judged by the status occupied by married women".<sup id="cite_ref-Oireachtas_116-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Oireachtas-116">[116]</a></sup> Until the 1970s, legal subordination of married women was common across European countries, through <a href="/wiki/Marriage_laws" class="mw-redirect" title="Marriage laws">marriage laws</a> giving legal authority to the husband, as well as through <a href="/wiki/Marriage_bar" title="Marriage bar">marriage bars</a>. In France, married women obtained the right to work without their husband's consent in 1965;<sup id="cite_ref-117" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-117">[117]</a></sup> while the paternal authority of a man over his family was ended in 1970 (before that parental responsibilities belonged solely to the father who made all legal decisions concerning the children); and a new reform in 1985 abolished the stipulation that the father had the sole power to administer the children's property.<sup id="cite_ref-118" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-118">[118]</a></sup> In <a href="/wiki/Austria" title="Austria">Austria</a>, the marriage law was overhauled between 1975 and 1983, abolishing the restrictions on married women's right to work outside the home, providing for equality between spouses, and for joint ownership of property and assets.<sup id="cite_ref-119" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-119">[119]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Switzerland" title="Switzerland">Switzerland</a> was one of the last countries in Europe to establish gender equality in marriage, in this country married women's rights were severely restricted until 1988, when legal reforms providing for gender equality in marriage, abolishing the legal authority of the husband, come into force (these reforms had been approved in 1985 by voters in a <a href="/wiki/Voting_in_Switzerland" title="Voting in Switzerland">referendum</a>, who narrowly voted in favor with 54.7% of voters approving).<sup id="cite_ref-120" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-120">[120]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-121" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-121">[121]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-122" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-122">[122]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-123" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-123">[123]</a></sup> In the <a href="/wiki/Netherlands" title="Netherlands">Netherlands</a>, although the legal incapacity of a married woman was abolished in 1956, the marriage bar for women civil servants being lifted in 1957, it was only in 1984 that full legal equality between husband and wife was achieved - prior to 1984 the law stipulated that the husband's opinion prevailed over the wife's regarding issues such as decisions on children's education and the domicile of the family.<sup id="cite_ref-124" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-124">[124]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-125" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-125">[125]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-126" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-126">[126]</a></sup> In 1978, the <a href="/wiki/Council_of_Europe" title="Council of Europe">Council of Europe</a> passed the <i>Resolution (78) 37 on equality of spouses in civil law</i>.<sup id="cite_ref-127" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-127">[127]</a></sup></p>
<p>In the <a href="/wiki/United_States" title="United States">United States</a>, a wife's legal subordination to her husband was fully ended by the case of <i><a href="/wiki/Kirchberg_v._Feenstra" title="Kirchberg v. Feenstra">Kirchberg v. Feenstra</a></i>, <a href="/wiki/List_of_United_States_Supreme_Court_cases,_volume_450" title="List of United States Supreme Court cases, volume 450">450</a> <a href="/wiki/United_States_Reports" title="United States Reports">U.S.</a> 455 (1981), a <a href="/wiki/Supreme_Court_of_the_United_States" title="Supreme Court of the United States">United States Supreme Court</a> case in which the Court held a <a href="/wiki/Louisiana" title="Louisiana">Louisiana</a> <a href="/wiki/Head_and_Master_law" title="Head and Master law">Head and Master law</a>, which gave sole control of marital property to the husband, unconstitutional.<sup id="cite_ref-128" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-128">[128]</a></sup></p>
<p>There have been and sometimes continue to be unequal treatment of married women in various aspects of everyday life. For example, in <a href="/wiki/Australia" title="Australia">Australia</a>, until 1983 a husband had to authorize a passport application for a married woman.<sup id="cite_ref-129" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-129">[129]</a></sup> Other practices have included, and in many countries continue to include, a requirement for a husband's consent for an application for bank loans and credit cards by a married woman, as well as restrictions on the wife's <a href="/wiki/Reproductive_rights" title="Reproductive rights">reproductive rights</a>, such as a requirement that the husband consents to the wife's acquiring of <a href="/wiki/Contraception" class="mw-redirect" title="Contraception">contraception</a> or having an <a href="/wiki/Abortion" title="Abortion">abortion</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-130" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-130">[130]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-131" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-131">[131]</a></sup> In some places, although the law itself no longer requires the consent of the husband for various actions taken by the wife, the practice continues <i><a href="/wiki/De_facto" title="De facto">de facto</a></i>, with the authorization of the husband being asked in practice.<sup id="cite_ref-132" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-132">[132]</a></sup></p>
<p>Although <a href="/wiki/Dowry" title="Dowry">dowry</a> is today associated with <a href="/wiki/South_Asia" title="South Asia">South Asia</a>, the practice has been common until the mid-20th century in parts of <a href="/wiki/Southeast_Europe" title="Southeast Europe">Southeast Europe</a>. For example, in <a href="/wiki/Greece" title="Greece">Greece</a> dowry was removed from <a href="/wiki/Family_law" title="Family law">family law</a> only in 1983 through legal changes which reformed marriage law and provided gender equality in marriage.<sup id="cite_ref-133" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-133">[133]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-134" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-134">[134]</a></sup> These changes also dealt with the practice of women <a href="/wiki/Married_and_maiden_names" class="mw-redirect" title="Married and maiden names">changing their surnames to that of the husbands</a> upon getting married, a practice which has been outlawed or restricted in some jurisdictions, because it is seen as contrary to women's rights. As such, women in Greece are required to <i>keep</i> their birth names for their whole life.<sup id="cite_ref-135" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-135">[135]</a></sup></p>
<p>Laws regulating marriage and divorce continue to discriminate against women in many countries. For example, in <a href="/wiki/Yemen" title="Yemen">Yemen</a>, marriage regulations state that a wife must obey her husband and must not leave home without his permission.<sup id="cite_ref-www2.ohchr.org_70-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-www2.ohchr.org-70">[70]</a></sup> In <a href="/wiki/Iraq" title="Iraq">Iraq</a> husbands have a legal right to "punish" their wives, with paragraph 41 of the criminal code stating that there is no crime if an act is committed while exercising a legal right. Examples of legal rights include: "The punishment of a wife by her husband, the disciplining by parents and teachers of children under their authority within certain limits prescribed by law or by custom".<sup id="cite_ref-136" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-136">[136]</a></sup> In the 1990s and the 21st century there has been progress in many countries in Africa: for instance in Namibia the <a href="/wiki/Marital_power" title="Marital power">marital power</a> of the husband was abolished in 1996 by the <i>Married Persons Equality Act</i>; in Botswana it was abolished in 2004 by the <i>Abolition of Marital Power Act</i>; and in Lesotho it was abolished in 2006 by the <i>Married Persons Equality Act</i>.<sup id="cite_ref-137" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-137">[137]</a></sup></p>
<p>Violence and mistreatment of women in relation to marriage has come to international attention during the past decades. This includes both violence committed inside marriage (<a href="/wiki/Domestic_violence" title="Domestic violence">domestic violence</a>) as well as violence related to marriage customs and traditions (such as <a href="/wiki/Dowry" title="Dowry">dowry</a>, <a href="/wiki/Bride_price" title="Bride price">bride price</a>, <a href="/wiki/Forced_marriage" title="Forced marriage">forced marriage</a> and <a href="/wiki/Child_marriage" title="Child marriage">child marriage</a>). Violence against a wife continues to be seen as legally acceptable in some countries; for instance in 2010, the United Arab Emirates's Supreme Court ruled that a man has the right to physically discipline his wife and children as long as he does not leave physical marks.<sup id="cite_ref-138" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-138">[138]</a></sup> The criminalization of <a href="/wiki/Adultery" title="Adultery">adultery</a> has been criticized as being a prohibition, which, in law or in practice, is used primarily against women; and incites violence against women (<a href="/wiki/Crimes_of_passion" class="mw-redirect" title="Crimes of passion">crimes of passion</a>, honor killings). A Joint Statement by the United Nations Working Group on discrimination against women in law and in practice in 2012 stated:<sup id="cite_ref-139" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-139">[139]</a></sup> "the United Nations Working Group on discrimination against women in law and in practice is deeply concerned at the criminalization and penalization of adultery whose enforcement leads to discrimination and violence against women." UN Women also stated that "Drafters should repeal any criminal offenses related to adultery or extramarital sex between consenting adults".<sup id="cite_ref-140" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-140">[140]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Investigation_and_prosecution_of_crimes_against_women_and_girls">Investigation and prosecution of crimes against women and girls</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Gender_equality&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Investigation and prosecution of crimes against women and girls">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Human rights organizations have expressed concern about the legal <a href="/wiki/Impunity" title="Impunity">impunity</a> of perpetrators of crimes against women, with such crimes being often ignored by authorities.<sup id="cite_ref-141" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-141">[141]</a></sup> This is especially the case with murders of women in <a href="/wiki/Latin_America" title="Latin America">Latin America</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-142" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-142">[142]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-143" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-143">[143]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-144" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-144">[144]</a></sup> In particular, there is impunity in regard to <a href="/wiki/Domestic_violence" title="Domestic violence">domestic violence</a>. High Commissioner for Human Rights, <a href="/wiki/Navi_Pillay" title="Navi Pillay">Navi Pillay</a>, has stated on domestic violence against women:<sup id="cite_ref-145" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-145">[145]</a></sup></p>
<dl>
<dd>"The reality for most victims, including victims of honor killings, is that state institutions fail them and that most perpetrators of domestic violence can rely on a culture of impunity for the acts they commit – acts which would often be considered as crimes, and be punished as such, if they were committed against strangers."</dd>
</dl>
<p>Women are often, in law or in practice, unable to access legal institutions. UN Women has said that: "Too often, justice institutions, including the police and the courts, deny women justice".<sup id="cite_ref-146" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-146">[146]</a></sup> Often, women are denied legal recourse because the state institutions themselves are structured and operate in ways incompatible with genuine justice for women who experience violence - according to Amnesty International, "Women who are victims of gender-related violence often have little recourse because many state agencies are themselves guilty of gender bias and discriminatory practices."<sup id="cite_ref-147" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-147">[147]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Gender_stereotypes">Gender stereotypes</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Gender_equality&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Gender stereotypes">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Bettie_Page_driving.jpg" class="image"><img alt="Series of photographs lampooning women drivers" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a6/Bettie_Page_driving.jpg/300px-Bettie_Page_driving.jpg" width="300" height="205" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a6/Bettie_Page_driving.jpg/450px-Bettie_Page_driving.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a6/Bettie_Page_driving.jpg/600px-Bettie_Page_driving.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1758" data-file-height="1202" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Bettie_Page_driving.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
1952 portrayal of stereotypes about women drivers, based on the stereotype that women can't drive well. Features <a href="/wiki/Bettie_Page" title="Bettie Page">Bettie Page</a>.</div>
</div>
</div>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Gender_role" title="Gender role">Gender role</a></div>
<p>Gender <a href="/wiki/Stereotype" title="Stereotype">stereotypes</a> arise from the socially approved roles of women and men in the private or public sphere, at home or in the workplace.In the household, women are typically seen as mother figures, which usually places them into a typical classification of being "supportive" or "nurturing". Women are expected to want to take on the role of a mother and take on primary responsibility for household needs.<sup id="cite_ref-148" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-148">[148]</a></sup> Their male counterparts are seen as being "assertive" or "ambitious" as men are usually seen in the workplace or as the primary breadwinner for his family. <sup id="cite_ref-149" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-149">[149]</a></sup> Due to these views and expectations, women often face discrimination in the public sphere, such as the workplace. <sup id="cite_ref-150" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-150">[150]</a></sup></p>
<p>A <a href="/wiki/Gender_role" title="Gender role">gender role</a> is a set of societal <a href="/wiki/Norm_(social)" title="Norm (social)">norms</a> dictating the types of behaviors which are generally considered acceptable, appropriate, or desirable for people based on their sex. Gender roles are usually centered on conceptions of <a href="/wiki/Femininity" title="Femininity">femininity</a> and <a href="/wiki/Masculinity" title="Masculinity">masculinity</a>, although there are <a href="/wiki/Third_gender" title="Third gender">exceptions</a> and <a href="/wiki/Genderqueer" title="Genderqueer">variations</a>. The <a href="/wiki/Convention_on_preventing_and_combating_violence_against_women_and_domestic_violence" title="Convention on preventing and combating violence against women and domestic violence">Istanbul Convention</a> contains a definition of "gender", stating that: "“gender” shall mean the socially constructed roles, behaviours, activities and attributes that a given society considers appropriate for women and men". (Article 3–Definitions (c))<sup id="cite_ref-151" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-151">[151]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Harmful_traditional_practices">Harmful traditional practices</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Gender_equality&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Harmful traditional practices">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Say_no_to_dowry.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7b/Say_no_to_dowry.jpg/220px-Say_no_to_dowry.jpg" width="220" height="342" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7b/Say_no_to_dowry.jpg/330px-Say_no_to_dowry.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7b/Say_no_to_dowry.jpg/440px-Say_no_to_dowry.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1613" data-file-height="2511" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Say_no_to_dowry.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Anti-dowry poster in <a href="/wiki/Bangalore,_India" class="mw-redirect" title="Bangalore, India">Bangalore, India</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:FGM_prevalence_UNICEF_2013.svg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/51/FGM_prevalence_UNICEF_2013.svg/220px-FGM_prevalence_UNICEF_2013.svg.png" width="220" height="177" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/51/FGM_prevalence_UNICEF_2013.svg/330px-FGM_prevalence_UNICEF_2013.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/51/FGM_prevalence_UNICEF_2013.svg/440px-FGM_prevalence_UNICEF_2013.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="1548" data-file-height="1248" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:FGM_prevalence_UNICEF_2013.svg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Prevalence of FGM by country, according to a 2013 UNICEF report<sup id="cite_ref-childinfo_152-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-childinfo-152">[152]</a></sup></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>"Harmful traditional practices" refer to forms of violence which are committed in certain communities often enough to become cultural practice, and accepted for that reason. Young women are the main victims of such acts, although men can be affected.<sup id="cite_ref-NHS_Traditional_153-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NHS_Traditional-153">[153]</a></sup> They occur in an environment where women and girls have unequal rights and opportunities.<sup id="cite_ref-ohchr.org_154-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ohchr.org-154">[154]</a></sup> These practices include, according to the <a href="/wiki/Office_of_the_United_Nations_High_Commissioner_for_Human_Rights" title="Office of the United Nations High Commissioner for Human Rights">Office of the United Nations High Commissioner for Human Rights</a>:<sup id="cite_ref-ohchr.org_154-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ohchr.org-154">[154]</a></sup></p>
<dl>
<dd>"<a href="/wiki/Female_genital_mutilation" title="Female genital mutilation">female genital mutilation</a> (FGM); forced feeding of women; early marriage; the various taboos or practices which prevent women from controlling their own fertility; nutritional taboos and traditional birth practices; son preference and its implications for the status of the girl child; female infanticide; early pregnancy; and dowry price"</dd>
</dl>
<p>Female genital mutilation is defined as "procedures that intentionally alter or cause injury to the female genital organs for non-medical reasons".<sup id="cite_ref-who.int_155-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-who.int-155">[155]</a></sup> An estimated 125 million women and girls living today have undergone FGM in the 29 countries where data exist. Of these, about half live in two countries, Egypt and Ethiopia.<sup id="cite_ref-156" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-156">[156]</a></sup> It is most commonly carried out on girls between infancy and 15 years old.<sup id="cite_ref-NHS_Traditional_153-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NHS_Traditional-153">[153]</a></sup></p>
<p><a href="/wiki/UNFPA" class="mw-redirect" title="UNFPA">UNFPA</a> and <a href="/wiki/UNICEF" title="UNICEF">UNICEF</a> state that, "In every society where it is practiced, FGM is a manifestation of deeply entrenched gender inequality. It persists for many reasons. In some societies, for example, it is considered a rite of passage. In others, it is seen as a prerequisite for marriage. In some communities, whether Christian, Jewish, Muslim, the practice may even be attributed to religious beliefs. Because FGM may be considered an important part of a culture or identity, it can be difficult for families to decide against having their daughters cut. People who reject the practice may face condemnation or ostracism. Even parents who do not want their daughters to undergo FGM may feel compelled to participate in the practice."<sup id="cite_ref-UNFPA-UNICEF_Joint_Programme_Report_on_Female_Genital_Mutilation.2FCutting:_Accelerating_Change_157-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-UNFPA-UNICEF_Joint_Programme_Report_on_Female_Genital_Mutilation.2FCutting:_Accelerating_Change-157">[157]</a></sup></p>
<p>Son preference refers to a cultural preference for sons over daughters, and manifests itself through practices such as sex selective abortion; female infanticide; or abandonment, neglect or abuse of girl-children.<sup id="cite_ref-ohchr.org_154-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ohchr.org-154">[154]</a></sup></p>
<p>Early marriage, child marriage or <a href="/wiki/Forced_marriage" title="Forced marriage">forced marriage</a> is prevalent in parts of Asia and Africa. The majority of victims seeking advice are female and aged between 18 and 23.<sup id="cite_ref-NHS_Traditional_153-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NHS_Traditional-153">[153]</a></sup> Such marriages can have harmful effects on a girl's education and development, and may expose girls to social isolation or abuse.<sup id="cite_ref-ohchr.org_154-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ohchr.org-154">[154]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-158" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-158">[158]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-159" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-159">[159]</a></sup></p>
<p>The 2013 UN Resolution on Child, Early and Forced Marriage calls for an end to the practice, and states that "Recognizing that child, early and forced marriage is a harmful practice that violates abuses, or impairs human rights and is linked to and perpetuates other harmful practices and human rights violations, that these violations have a disproportionately negative impact on women and girls [...]".<sup id="cite_ref-160" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-160">[160]</a></sup> Despite a near-universal commitment by governments to end child marriage, "one in three girls in developing countries (excluding China) will probably be married before they are 18."<sup id="cite_ref-unfpa.org2_161-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-unfpa.org2-161">[161]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/UNFPA" class="mw-redirect" title="UNFPA">UNFPA</a> states that, "over 67 million women 20-24 year old in 2010 had been married as girls. Half were in Asia, one-fifth in Africa. In the next decade 14.2 million girls under 18 will be married every year; this translates into 39,000 girls married each day. This will rise to an average of 15.1 million girls a year, starting in 2021 until 2030, if present trends continue."<sup id="cite_ref-unfpa.org2_161-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-unfpa.org2-161">[161]</a></sup></p>
<p>Abuses regarding nutrition are taboos in regard to certain foods, which result in poor nutrition of women, and may endanger their health, especially if pregnant.<sup id="cite_ref-ohchr.org_154-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ohchr.org-154">[154]</a></sup></p>
<p>Women's ability to control their fertility is often reduced. For instance, in northern Ghana, the payment of <a href="/wiki/Bride_price" title="Bride price">bride price</a> signifies a woman's requirement to bear children, and women using birth control face threats, violence and reprisals.<sup id="cite_ref-162" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-162">[162]</a></sup> Births in parts of Africa are often attended by traditional birth attendants (TBAs), who sometimes perform rituals that are dangerous to the health of the mother. In many societies, a difficult labour is believed to be a divine punishment for marital infidelity, and such women face abuse and are pressured to "confess" to the infidelity.<sup id="cite_ref-ohchr.org_154-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ohchr.org-154">[154]</a></sup> The custom of bride price has been criticized as contributing to the mistreatment of women in marriage, and preventing them from leaving abusive marriages. <a href="/wiki/UN_Women" title="UN Women">UN Women</a> recommended its abolition, and stated that: "Legislation should [...] State that divorce shall not be contingent upon the return of bride price but such provisions shall not be interpreted to limit women’s right to divorce; State that a perpetrator of <a href="/wiki/Domestic_violence" title="Domestic violence">domestic violence</a>, including marital rape, cannot use the fact that he paid bride price as a defence to a domestic violence charge."<sup id="cite_ref-ReferenceB_52-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ReferenceB-52">[52]</a></sup></p>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Caste_system_in_India" title="Caste system in India">caste system in India</a> which leads to <a href="/wiki/Untouchability" title="Untouchability">untouchability</a> (the practice of ostracizing a group by segregating them from the mainstream society) often interacts with gender discrimination, leading to a double discrimination faced by <a href="/wiki/Dalit" title="Dalit">Dalit</a> women.<sup id="cite_ref-163" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-163">[163]</a></sup> In a 2014 survey, 27% of Indians admitted to practicing untouchability.<sup id="cite_ref-164" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-164">[164]</a></sup></p>
<p>Tribal traditions can be harmful to males; for instance, the <a href="/wiki/Satere-Mawe" class="mw-redirect" title="Satere-Mawe">Satere-Mawe</a> tribe use <a href="/wiki/Bullet_ant" class="mw-redirect" title="Bullet ant">bullet ants</a> as an <a href="/wiki/Initiation_rite" class="mw-redirect" title="Initiation rite">initiation rite</a>. Men must wear gloves with hundreds of bullet ants woven in for ten minutes: the ants' stings cause severe pain and paralysis. This experience must be completed twenty times for boys to be considered "warriors".<sup id="cite_ref-165" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-165">[165]</a></sup></p>
<p>Other harmful traditional practices include <a href="/wiki/Marriage_by_abduction" class="mw-redirect" title="Marriage by abduction">marriage by abduction</a>, ritualized <a href="/wiki/Sexual_slavery" title="Sexual slavery">sexual slavery</a> (<a href="/wiki/Devadasi" title="Devadasi">Devadasi</a>, <a href="/wiki/Trokosi" class="mw-redirect" title="Trokosi">Trokosi</a>), <a href="/wiki/Breast_ironing" title="Breast ironing">breast ironing</a> and <a href="/wiki/Widow_inheritance" title="Widow inheritance">widow inheritance</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-166" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-166">[166]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-167" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-167">[167]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-168" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-168">[168]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-169" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-169">[169]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Portrayal_of_women_in_the_media">Portrayal of women in the media</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Gender_equality&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Portrayal of women in the media">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Exploitation_of_women_in_mass_media" title="Exploitation of women in mass media">Exploitation of women in mass media</a></div>
<p>The way women are represented in the media has been criticized as interfering with the aim of achieving gender equality by perpetuating negative gender stereotypes. The <a href="/wiki/Exploitation_of_women_in_mass_media" title="Exploitation of women in mass media">exploitation of women in mass media</a> refers to the criticisms that are levied against the use or objectification of women in the <a href="/wiki/Mass_media" title="Mass media">mass media</a>, when such use or portrayal aims at increasing the appeal of media or a product, to the detriment of, or without regard to, the interests of the women portrayed, or women in general. Concerns include the fact that all forms of media have the power to shape the population's perceptions and portray images of unrealistic stereotypical perceptions that falsely implies that women are unimportant or invisible. Criticisms of the way women are represented in the media is that the media reinforces stereotypical societal views of "what women are for", by portraying women either as submissive housewives or as sex objects.<sup id="cite_ref-170" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-170">[170]</a></sup> The media emphasizes traditional domestic or sexual roles that normalize violence against women.</p>
<p>According to a study, the way women are often portrayed by the media can lead to: "Women of average or normal appearance feeling inadequate or less beautiful in comparison to the overwhelming use of extraordinarily attractive women"; "Increase in the likelihood and acceptance of sexual violence"; "Unrealistic expectations by men of how women should look or behave"; "Psychological disorders such as body dysmorphic disorder, anorexia, bulimia and so on"; "The importance of physical appearance is emphasized and reinforced early in most girls' development." Studies have found that nearly half of females ages 6–8 have stated they want to be slimmer. (Striegel-Moore &amp; Franko, 2002)".<sup id="cite_ref-171" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-171">[171]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-172" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-172">[172]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Health">Health</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Gender_equality&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Health">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Gender_disparities_in_health" title="Gender disparities in health">Gender disparities in health</a></div>
<p>Social constructs of <a href="/wiki/Gender" title="Gender">gender</a> (that is, cultural ideals of socially acceptable <a href="/wiki/Masculinity" title="Masculinity">masculinity</a> and <a href="/wiki/Femininity" title="Femininity">femininity</a>) often have a negative effect on health. The WHO cites the example of women not being allowed to travel alone outside the home (to go to the hospital), and women being prevented by cultural norms to ask their husbands to use a condom, in cultures which simultaneously encourage male promiscuity, as social norms that harm <a href="/wiki/Women%27s_health" title="Women's health">women's health</a>. Teenage boys suffering accidents due to social expectations of impressing their peers through <a href="/wiki/Risk" title="Risk">risk taking</a>, and men dying at much higher rate from <a href="/wiki/Lung_cancer" title="Lung cancer">lung cancer</a> due to <a href="/wiki/Smoking" title="Smoking">smoking</a>, in cultures which link smoking to masculinity, are cited by the WHO as examples of gender norms negatively affecting <a href="/wiki/Men%27s_health" title="Men's health">men's health</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-173" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-173">[173]</a></sup> The WHO has also stated that there is a strong connection between gender socialization and transmission and lack of adequate management of <a href="/wiki/HIV/AIDS" title="HIV/AIDS">HIV/AIDS</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-ReferenceA_55-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ReferenceA-55">[55]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Informing_women_of_their_rights">Informing women of their rights</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Gender_equality&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Informing women of their rights">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>While in many countries, the problem lies in the lack of adequate legislation, in others the principal problem is not as much the lack of a legal framework, but the fact is that most women do not know their legal rights. This is especially the case as many of the laws dealing with women's rights are of recent date. This lack of knowledge enables to abusers to lead the victims (explicitly or implicitly) to believe that their abuse is within their rights. This may apply to a wide range of abuses, ranging from domestic violence to employment discrimination.<sup id="cite_ref-174" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-174">[174]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-175" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-175">[175]</a></sup> The United Nations Development Programme states that, in order to advance gender justice, "Women must know their rights and be able to access legal systems".<sup id="cite_ref-176" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-176">[176]</a></sup></p>
<p>The 1993 UN <a href="/wiki/Declaration_on_the_Elimination_of_Violence_Against_Women" title="Declaration on the Elimination of Violence Against Women">Declaration on the Elimination of Violence Against Women</a> states at Art. 4 (d) [...] "States should also inform women of their rights in seeking redress through such mechanisms".<sup id="cite_ref-177" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-177">[177]</a></sup> Enacting protective legislation against violence has little effect, if women do not know how to use it: for example a study of Bedouin women in Israel found that 60% did not know what a <a href="/wiki/Restraining_order" title="Restraining order">restraining order</a> was;<sup id="cite_ref-178" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-178">[178]</a></sup> or if they don't know what acts are illegal: a report by Amnesty International showed in Hungary, in a public opinion poll of nearly 1,200 people in 2006, a total of 62% did not know that marital rape was an illegal (it was outlawed in 1997) and therefore the crime was rarely reported.<sup id="cite_ref-179" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-179">[179]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-180" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-180">[180]</a></sup> Ensuring women have a minim understanding of health issues is also important: lack of access to reliable medical information and available medical procedures to which they are entitled hurts <a href="/wiki/Women%27s_health" title="Women's health">women's health</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-181" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-181">[181]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Gender_mainstreaming">Gender mainstreaming</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Gender_equality&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Gender mainstreaming">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Gender_mainstreaming" title="Gender mainstreaming">Gender mainstreaming</a></div>
<p>Gender mainstreaming is described as the <a href="/wiki/Public_policy" title="Public policy">public policy</a> of assessing the different implications for women and men of any planned <a href="/wiki/Policy" title="Policy">policy</a> action, including <a href="/wiki/Legislation" title="Legislation">legislation</a> and <a href="/wiki/Program_(management)" class="mw-redirect" title="Program (management)">programmes</a>, in all areas and levels, with the aim of achieving gender equality.<sup id="cite_ref-182" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-182">[182]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-183" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-183">[183]</a></sup> The concept of gender mainstreaming was first proposed at the 1985 Third World Conference on Women in <a href="/wiki/Nairobi" title="Nairobi">Nairobi</a>, <a href="/wiki/Kenya" title="Kenya">Kenya</a>. The idea has been developed in the <a href="/wiki/United_Nations" title="United Nations">United Nations</a> development community.<sup id="cite_ref-184" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-184">[184]</a></sup> Gender mainstreaming "involves ensuring that gender perspectives and attention to the goal of gender equality are central to all activities".<sup id="cite_ref-185" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-185">[185]</a></sup></p>
<p>According to the Council of Europe definition: "Gender mainstreaming is the (re)organization, improvement, development and evaluation of policy processes, so that a gender equality perspective is incorporated in all policies at all levels and at all stages, by the actors normally involved in policy-making."<sup id="cite_ref-coe.int_104-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-coe.int-104">[104]</a></sup></p>
<p>An integrated gender mainstreaming approach is "the attempt to form alliances and common platforms that bring together the power of faith and gender-equality aspirations to advance human rights."<sup id="cite_ref-unfpa.org3_186-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-unfpa.org3-186">[186]</a></sup> For example, "in Azerbaijan, <a href="/wiki/UNFPA" class="mw-redirect" title="UNFPA">UNFPA</a> conducted a study on gender equality by comparing the text of the Convention on the Elimination of All Forms of Discrimination against Women with some widely recognized Islamic references and resources. The results reflect the parallels between the Convention and many tenets of Islamic scripture and practice. The study showcased specific issues, including VAW, child marriage, respect for the dignity of women, and equality in the economic and political participation of women. The study was later used to produce training materials geared towards sensitizing religious leaders."<sup id="cite_ref-unfpa.org3_186-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-unfpa.org3-186">[186]</a></sup></p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="References">References</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Gender_equality&amp;action=edit&amp;section=28" title="Edit section: References">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="reflist columns references-column-width" style="-moz-column-width: 30em; -webkit-column-width: 30em; column-width: 30em; list-style-type: decimal;">
<ol class="references">
<li id="cite_note-1"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-1">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">United Nations. Report of the Economic and Social Council for 1997. A/52/3.18 September 1997, at 28: "Mainstreaming a gender perspective is the process of assessing the implications for women and men of any planned action, including legislation, policies or programmes, in all areas and at all levels. It is a strategy for making women's as well as men's concerns and experiences an integral dimension of the design, implementation, monitoring and evaluation of policies and programmes in all political, economic and societal spheres so that women and men benefit equally and inequality is not perpetuated. The ultimate goal is to achieve gender equality."</span></li>

<li id="cite_note-15"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-15">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><cite id="CITEREFMcKieHearn2004" class="citation journal">McKie, Linda; Hearn, Jeff (August 2004). <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://dx.doi.org/10.3366/scot.2004.0043">"Gender-neutrality and gender equality: comparing and contrasting policy responses to 'domestic violence' in Finland and Scotland"</a>. <i><a href="/wiki/Scottish_Affairs" title="Scottish Affairs">Scottish Affairs</a></i>. <a href="/wiki/Edinburgh_University_Press" title="Edinburgh University Press">Edinburgh University Press</a>. <b>48</b> (1): 85–107. <a href="/wiki/Digital_object_identifier" title="Digital object identifier">doi</a>:<a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="//dx.doi.org/10.3366%2Fscot.2004.0043">10.3366/scot.2004.0043</a>.</cite><span title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AGender+equality&amp;rft.atitle=Gender-neutrality+and+gender+equality%3A+comparing+and+contrasting+policy+responses+to+%27domestic+violence%27+in+Finland+and+Scotland&amp;rft.aufirst=Linda&amp;rft.au=Hearn%2C+Jeff&amp;rft.aulast=McKie&amp;rft.date=2004-08&amp;rft.genre=article&amp;rft_id=http%3A%2F%2Fdx.doi.org%2F10.3366%2Fscot.2004.0043&amp;rft_id=info%3Adoi%2F10.3366%2Fscot.2004.0043&amp;rft.issue=1&amp;rft.jtitle=Scottish+Affairs&amp;rft.pages=85-107&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Ajournal&amp;rft.volume=48" class="Z3988"><span style="display:none;">&#160;</span></span> <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://web.archive.org/web/20131021023708/http://www.scottishaffairs.org/backiss/pdfs/sa48/sa48_McKie_and_Hearn.pdf">Pdf.</a></span></li>
<li id="cite_note-Amnesty_Finland-16"><span class="mw-cite-backlink">^ <a href="#cite_ref-Amnesty_Finland_16-0"><sup><i><b>a</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-Amnesty_Finland_16-1"><sup><i><b>b</b></i></sup></a></span> <span class="reference-text"><cite id="CITEREFDanish.2C_Swedish.2C_Finnish_and_Norwegian_sections_of_Amnesty_International2010" class="citation">Danish, Swedish, Finnish and Norwegian sections of Amnesty International (March 2010), "Rape and human rights in Finland", in Danish, Swedish, Finnish and Norwegian sections of Amnesty International, <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://humanrightshouse.org/Articles/13618.html"><i>Case closed: rape and human rights in the Nordic countries</i></a>, <a href="/wiki/Amnesty_International" title="Amnesty International">Amnesty International</a>, pp.&#160;89–91, <q><i>Finland is repeatedly reminded of its widespread problem of violence against women and recommended to take more efficient measures to deal with the situation. International criticism concentrates on the lack of measures to combat violence against women in general and in particular on the lack of a national action plan to combat such violence and on the lack of legislation on domestic violence. (...) Compared to Sweden, Finland has been slower to reform legislation on violence against women. In Sweden, domestic violence was already illegal in 1864, while in Finland such violence was not outlawed until 1970, over a hundred years later. In Sweden the punishment of victims of incest was abolished in 1937 but not until 1971 in Finland. Rape within marriage was criminalised in Sweden in 1962, but the equivalent Finnish legislation only came into force in 1994 — making Finland one of the last European countries to criminalise marital rape. In addition, assaults taking place on private property did not become impeachable offences in Finland until 1995. Only in 1997 did victims of sexual offences and domestic violence in Finland become entitled to government-funded counselling and support services for the duration of their court cases.</i></q></cite><span title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AGender+equality&amp;rft.atitle=Rape+and+human+rights+in+Finland&amp;rft.au=Danish%2C+Swedish%2C+Finnish+and+Norwegian+sections+of+Amnesty+International&amp;rft.btitle=Case+closed%3A+rape+and+human+rights+in+the+Nordic+countries&amp;rft.date=2010-03&amp;rft.genre=bookitem&amp;rft_id=http%3A%2F%2Fhumanrightshouse.org%2FArticles%2F13618.html&amp;rft.pages=89-91&amp;rft.pub=Amnesty+International&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook" class="Z3988"><span style="display:none;">&#160;</span></span> <span class="citation-comment" style="display:none; color:#33aa33">CS1 maint: Multiple names: authors list (<a href="/wiki/Category:CS1_maint:_Multiple_names:_authors_list" title="Category:CS1 maint: Multiple names: authors list">link</a>)</span> <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://amnesty.dk/media/1557/case-closed.pdf">Pdf.</a></span></li>
<li id="cite_note-17"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-17">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><cite class="citation book"><a href="/wiki/Amnesty_International" title="Amnesty International">Amnesty International</a> (May 2011). <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://www2.ohchr.org/english/bodies/hrc/docs/ngos/AI_Denmark_November2010.pdf"><i>Denmark: human rights violations and concerns in the context of counter-terrorism, immigration-detention, forcible return of rejected asylum-seekers and violence against women</i></a> <span style="font-size:85%;">(pdf)</span>. <a href="/wiki/Amnesty_International" title="Amnesty International">Amnesty International</a>.</cite><span title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AGender+equality&amp;rft.au=Amnesty+International&amp;rft.btitle=Denmark%3A+human+rights+violations+and+concerns+in+the+context+of+counter-terrorism%2C+immigration-detention%2C+forcible+return+of+rejected+asylum-seekers+and+violence+against+women&amp;rft.date=2011-05&amp;rft.genre=book&amp;rft_id=http%3A%2F%2Fwww2.ohchr.org%2Fenglish%2Fbodies%2Fhrc%2Fdocs%2Fngos%2FAI_Denmark_November2010.pdf&amp;rft.pub=Amnesty+International&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook" class="Z3988"><span style="display:none;">&#160;</span></span> Amnesty International submission to the UN Universal Periodic Review, May 2011.</span></li>

<li id="cite_note-32"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-32">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Although married women in France obtained the right to work without their husbands' permission in 1965,<cite class="citation web"><a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://web.archive.org/web/20160304092212/http://www.tandf.co.uk/journals/explore/cmcf-vsi-women-in-france.pdf">"Archived copy"</a> <span style="font-size:85%;">(PDF)</span>. Retrieved <span class="nowrap">2016-04-03</span></span>.</cite><span title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AGender+equality&amp;rft.btitle=Archived+copy&amp;rft.genre=unknown&amp;rft_id=http%3A%2F%2Fwww.tandf.co.uk%2Fjournals%2Fexplore%2Fcmcf-vsi-women-in-france.pdf&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook" class="Z3988"><span style="display:none;">&#160;</span></span> and the paternal authority of a man over his family was ended in 1970 (before that parental responsibilities belonged solely to the father who made all legal decisions concerning the children), it was only in 1985 that a legal reform abolished the stipulation that the husband had the sole power to administer the children's property. <a rel="nofollow" class="external autonumber" href="http://ceflonline.net/wp-content/uploads/France-Parental-Responsibilities.pdf">[4]</a></span></li>
